The Two Aspects of Hamd in Hanafi Commentary
Explaining why the author detailed hamd as 'first' and 'second'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the commentator distinguished hamd as 'first' (essential perfection) and 'second' (blessings) and identify the Quranic references that support this distinction.

Core Idea
Hamd is for Allah’s essence and for His blessings — the words 'first' and 'second' capture these two distinct aspects.

In this passage, the commentator responds to a potential grammatical confusion in the author's phrasing and then provides three reasons for the author's use of 'first' and 'second' when referring to hamd. This analysis reveals a deep structural reading of Surah al-Fatiha and other surahs, linking each phrase to a type of praise. Understanding this helps us appreciate how Hanafi scholars extract meaning from Quranic word order and repetition.

Guided Path
1
Resolve the grammatical issue
Identify the phrase from the old manuscript ('mujalliyan wa musalliyan yaqulu al-'abdu al-mutawassilu') and determine that 'hamidan' is a circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'yaqulu'.
This step clears the way for the commentator's main discussion.
2
Explain the first reason for 'first' and 'second'
Hamd can be for essential perfection (without receiving a blessing) or for blessings received. 'First' indicates praise for Allah's perfect essence and attributes; 'second' indicates praise for His blessings, including the success granted to the author.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi theology: hamd does not require a blessing to be valid.
3
Explain the second reason
Allah's blessings relate to creation and maintenance (ījād wa ibqā') in this world and the Hereafter, so hamd covers both. 'First' refers to the first creation/maintenance, 'second' to the second creation/maintenance.
This ties hamd to the two realms: dunya and akhirah.
4
Map to Surah al-Fatiha
Each phrase corresponds to a category: 'Rabbi al-'ālamīn' (first creation), 'al-Raḥmān al-Raḥīm' (first maintenance), 'Māliki yawmi al-dīn' (second creation), 'Iyyāka na'budu' (second maintenance).
This demonstrates how a single surah can encapsulate the entire scope of hamd.
5
Note the third reason (following Quranic surahs)
The author imitates the five surahs that begin with tahmid: Al-Fatiha, Al-An'am, Al-Kahf, etc.
This shows continuity with Quranic structure.

Source Text

عطا فرمائی اس لحاظ سے وہ ثانیا مستحق ہے تو اولاً کمال ذاتی پر حمد ہوا اور ثانیاً منعم اور انعامات عطا فرمانے کی وجہ سے استحقاق محمد ہوا پہلا بلا نعمت ثانی علی النعمۃ ہوا ۔ الثانى ان نعم الله تعالیٰ کے اندر ان جمیع اقسام کی طرف اشارہ کیا گیا ہے ایجاد اول کی طرف رب العالمین کے ساتھ اشارہ کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ اخراج من العدم الى الوجود اعظم تربیہ ہے اور الرحمن الرحیم کے ساتھ ابقار اول کی طرف اشارہ کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ ان کا مفہوم ہے المنعم سجلائل النعم ودقائقها کما ہو مذکور فی البیضاوی اور ان ہی نعمتوں پر نوع انسان کا بقافی الدنیا موقوف ہے اور مالک یوم الدین میں ایجاد ثانی کی طرف اشارہ ہے اور ایاک نعبد کے ساتھ ابھارثانی کی طرف اشارہ کیونکہ توحید اور عبادت کے جو منافع ہیں ان کا حصول فی الآخرت ہوگا جس کے نتیجہ میں وصول الی الجنہ ہو گا جو رحمتہ وسیعہ کا مظہر ہے ۔ دوسری سورۃ جس کی افتتاح بالتحمید ہے وہ انعام ہے اور تیسری کہف سورۃ انعام
As for the old version, the wording of the old manuscript is: 'mujalliyan wa musalliyan yaqulu al-'abdu al-mutawassilu'. In this version, there is no preventer, so it is apparent that 'hamidan' is a circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'al-qawl'. As for the explanation of 'Muhammad' with his saying 'first and second'—meaning the author detailed 'Muhammad' with 'first' and 'second'—the commentator has presented three reasons for this detail: First, that hamd is both upon His blessing and upon other than blessing, because in hamd, the arrival of blessing from the praised to the praiser is not a condition; just as hamd is given for a blessing that has arrived, similarly it is given without the arrival of the blessing. So with 'first' he indicated that Allah is deserving of hamd upon His perfect essence—upon His complete essence and great attributes—because Allah's essence in itself is complete and free from all defects and imperfections, and He is adorned with great attributes that indicate His essential perfection. In this regard, He is worthy firstly. And secondly, He is worthy because of the beautiful blessings and abundant favors that reach the servants from Him, and especially the favor upon the author that He granted him success in composing this book. In this regard, He is worthy secondly. So first hamd was for essential perfection, and second hamd was for being the Bestower of blessings and for granting favors. The first is without obligation, the second is upon obligation. Second, that the blessings of Allah, although numerous and infinite, their reference is to creation and maintenance—firstly, creation and maintenance in this world, and secondly, creation and maintenance in the Hereafter. So he is praising upon these two categories: with 'first' upon the first, and with 'second' upon the second. And in this, he is following and imitating the surahs of the Quran that begin with tahmid, which are five surahs. The first is Al-Fatiha, the mother of the Quran; in it, all these categories are indicated. 'Rabbi al-'alameen' indicates the first creation, because bringing into existence from nothing is the greatest nurturing. 'Al-Rahman al-Rahim' indicates the first maintenance, because their meaning is the Bestower of apparent and hidden blessings, as mentioned in Al-Baydawi. The survival of the human species in this world depends upon these blessings. 'Maliki yawmi al-din' indicates the second creation. 'Iyyaka na'budu' indicates the second maintenance, because the benefits of tawhid and worship will be obtained in the Hereafter, resulting in reaching Paradise, which is a manifestation of vast mercy. The second surah that begins with tahmid is Al-An'am, and the third is Al-Kahf, Surah Al-An'am.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is another part of the commentary on the author's introduction. Here, the commentator discusses two versions of the manuscript. In the old version, 'mujalliyan wa musalliyan yaqulu al-'abdu al-mutawassilu', there is no grammatical obstacle, so 'hamidan' can be read as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the agent of 'yaqulu'. Then the commentator explains why the author detailed 'muḥammad' (i.e., 'muḥammadan' in the original text) with the words 'awwalan' and 'thāniyan' (firstly and secondly). Three reasons are given: First, hamd can be for Allah's essential perfection (independent of any blessing) and also for His blessings. 'Awwalan' refers to hamd for His perfect essence and attributes, which He deserves in Himself. 'Thāniyan' refers to hamd for the blessings He bestows, including the specific blessing of authoring this book. Second, Allah's blessings relate to creation and maintenance (ījād wa ibqā') in this world and the Hereafter, so hamd covers both. The commentator then connects this analysis to Surah Al-Fatiha, where each phrase corresponds to one of these categories: 'Rabbi al-'ālamīn' (first creation), 'al-Raḥmān al-Raḥīm' (first maintenance), 'Māliki yawmi al-dīn' (second creation), and 'Iyyāka na'budu' (second maintenance). Other surahs beginning with tahmid are mentioned: Al-An'am, Al-Kahf. This is a sophisticated example of how Hanafi commentators read Quranic structure to reinforce theological and legal concepts.

Key Terms
الحمد (al-ḥamd): Praise directed to Allah either for His essential perfection or for His blessings.
الإيجاد (al-ījād): Creation or bringing into existence.
الإبقاء (al-ibqā'): Maintenance or preservation of existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first reason given in the passage, what does the term 'first' (awwalan) specifically refer to?
A) The initial creation of the world
B) Praise for Allah's perfect essence and attributes
C) The first blessing received by the author
D) The first of the five surahs beginning with tahmid

Key Takeaway
The two aspects of hamd reveal that praise of Allah is both for His perfect essence and for His blessings, and this is reflected in the structure of Surah al-Fatiha.

Defining Sharī‘ah: The Metaphor of Flowing Gardens
Defining shari‘ah and explaining metaphorical comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shari‘ah as used in Hanafi usul, explain the metaphorical comparison to gardens with water channels, and identify the types of metaphor (makkiyya, takhyiliyya, kinaya) used in the commentary.

Core Idea
Shari‘ah is the divine path of flowing mercy, like gardens irrigated by streams.

In this lesson, we delve into the definition of shari‘ah from the Hanafi usul perspective. The commentator not only provides the lexical meaning—'the religion legislated by Allah, made clear and apparent'—but also unpacks a rich metaphor that likens the shari‘ah to gardens with flowing water channels. This imagery is not merely poetic; it illustrates how the shari‘ah is a source of spiritual nourishment, drawing seekers to drink from its clear waters. Understanding this metaphor deepens our appreciation of the life-giving quality of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Define the term 'shari‘ah'
Locate the explicit definition in the passage: 'al-shar‘ and al-sharī‘a are synonyms, meaning the religion that Allah has legislated for His servants and made clear and apparent. Summary: the established way that is proven from the Messenger of Allah.' This is the core lexical definition used in Hanafi usul.
2
Identify the primary metaphor
The author states that the sharī‘a has been likened to gardens (rawḍāt) and paradises (jannāt) with water channels (mashāri‘). This is the central analogy: just as gardens have streams for irrigation, the shari‘ah has channels of divine mercy and guidance.
The word 'mashāri‘' (plural of mashra‘a) means drinking channels where water flows and people come to drink.
3
Classify the metaphorical devices used
The passage distinguishes between two types of metaphor: makkiyya (where the thing compared is implicit) and takhyīliyya (where accessories are explicitly mentioned and affirmed). The gardens themselves are the implicit comparison, while the channels are explicitly affirmed. Later, for the 'qabūl al-qabūl' example, a kināya metaphor is used (the east wind symbolizing acceptance).
Understanding these rhetorical categories helps in usul to interpret analogical arguments.
4
Apply the metaphor to the spiritual function of shari‘ah
Just as water channels bring life to gardens, the shari‘ah brings spiritual life to believers. The passage describes 'those thirsty for the clear water of Allah's mercy and pleasure' coming to these channels. This illustrates that the purpose of shari‘ah is to guide and purify.
5
Analyze the second metaphor: 'qabūl al-qabūl'
The author expands on his own previous metaphor about 'qabūl al-qabūl namār'. The first 'qabūl' means the east wind (al-ṣabā), and the second means acceptance of worship. The wind brings growth and rain, symbolising how divine acceptance brings spiritual tranquility and growth. This is another example of the author's rhetorical method.
The passage includes astronomical details about the east wind, showing the depth of the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من مشارع الشرع میں لفظ مشارع جمع ہے مشرعة المار کی یعنی وہ نالیاں جن کے اندر پانی جاری رہتا ہے اور شاربین وارد ہوتے رہتے ہیں۔ الشرع والشریعہ بمعنی واحد یعنی وہ دین جو اللہ تعالیٰ نے اپنے عباد کے لئے مشروع فرمایا اور اس کو واضح اور ظاہر کر دیا۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ طریقہ معہودہ جو ثابت ہے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم سے جعلها اى جعل الشریعہ یعنی شریعت کو مصنف نے روضات و جنات سے تشبیہ دی جو مضمر فی النفس ہے یہ استعارہ مکیہ ہوا چونکہ مشارع یعنی پانی پینے کی نالیاں بساتین و جنات کے لوازمات سے ہیں تو شریعت کے لئے ان کا اثبات بطور استعارہ تخیلیہ کے ہے یعنی جیسے روضات وجنات میں مشارع ہوتے ہیں جن پر شار بین وارد ہوتے ہیں اسی طرح شریعت مطہرہ کے لئے بھی مشارع ہیں جن پر اللہ تعالیٰ کی رحمت و رضوان کے زلال کے پیاسے وارد ہوتے رہتے ہیں۔ زلال الماء الصافی کو کہتے ہیں۔ وبهذا الطريق ال يعنى مصنف نے اپنے قول قبول القبول نمار میں بھی اسی طرح کے استعارے پیش کئے ہیں پہلے قبول سے مراد باد صبا ہے اور دوسرے قبول سے مراد قبولیت عبادت تو قبول عبادت کو تشبیہ دی جہب الریح کے ساتھ یعنی اس سمت وجہہ کے ساتھ جس سے ہوا چلتی ہے کیونکہ قبول عبادت بھی ایک مہب ہے جہاں سے الطاف رحمن اور انوار غفران چلتے ہیں اور ظاہر ہوتے ہیں اور یہ تشبیہ مضمر فی النفس ہے یہ استعارہ بالکنایہ ہو اور ریح الصبا یہ مہب الریح کے لوازمات سے ہے اس کا اثبات استعارہ تخیلیہ ہوا جیسے ریح الصبا کے ساتھ ابدان کی راحت اور اغصان کو نما حاصل ہوتا ہے اسی طرح صبار حمانی اور نور مغفرت کے ساتھ سکونِ قلب اور راحتِ دل حاصل ہوتی ہے اور ریح صبا عموما ان ایام میں چلتی ہے جب لیل و نہار آپس میں برابر ہو جاتے ہیں اور مطلع شمس یعنی مشرقی سمت سے چلتی ہے اور اس کے مقابل یعنی مغربی سمت سے جو ہوا چلتی ہے اسے دیور کہتے ہیں اہلِ عرب کا خیال ہے کہ دبوری ہوا بادل کو اٹھاتی ہے اور او سپر فضا میں لے جا کہ اس کو چلاتی ہے جب بادل اونچا ہو جاتا ہے تو ہٹ جاتی ہے پھر باد صبا اس بادل کا استقبال کرتی ہے اور مختلف ٹکڑوں کو ایک دوسرے پر لگا کہ ایک ہی ٹکڑا بنا لیتی ہے پھر بارش اترتی ہے جس کے ساتھ اشجار کا نشو نما ہوتا ہے قبول ثانی مصدر ہے لیکن ہے مصادر شازہ و
From 'mashāri‘ al-shar‘': the word 'mashāri‘' is the plural of 'mashra‘a' meaning the channels in which water flows and drinkers come to drink. Al-shar‘ and al-sharī‘a are synonyms, meaning the religion that Allah the Exalted has legislated for His servants and made clear and apparent. Summary: the established way that is proven from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. He, i.e. the author, likened the sharī‘a to gardens and paradises, which is implicit in the mind; this is a makkiyya metaphor. Since mashāri‘, i.e. drinking channels, are among the accessories of gardens and paradises, their affirmation for the sharī‘a is as a takhyīliyya metaphor. Just as gardens and paradises have channels on which drinkers come, so too does the pure sharī‘a have channels on which those thirsty for the clear water of Allah's mercy and pleasure come. 'Zulāl' refers to pure water. And by this method, the author also presented similar metaphors in his statement 'qabūl al-qabūl namār'. The first 'qabūl' means the east wind, and the second 'qabūl' means acceptance of worship. So he likened acceptance of worship to the direction from which the wind blows, because acceptance of worship is also a place where the favors of the Most Merciful and the lights of forgiveness blow and appear. This likening is implicit in the mind; it is a kināya metaphor. The east wind is from the accessories of the wind's direction, so its affirmation is a takhyīliyya metaphor. Just as with the east wind the bodies find comfort and branches grow, similarly with divine patience and light of forgiveness, tranquility of heart and comfort of soul are attained. The east wind generally blows during the days when night and day become equal, and it blows from the east direction. The wind that blows from the opposite direction, i.e. the west, is called 'dabūr'. The Arabs believe that the west wind lifts clouds and drives them into the upper atmosphere; when the cloud rises high, it moves away, then the east wind receives the cloud and attaches different pieces to each other, making them one piece; then rain falls, with which trees grow. The second 'qabūl' is a verbal noun, but it is among irregular verbal nouns.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sharī‘ah' and explains the author's metaphorical use of related imagery. The word 'mashāri‘' (water channels) is derived from 'shar‘', emphasizing that Islamic law is like a source of flowing water where seekers come to drink. The author uses a metaphor likening the sharī‘ah to gardens with streams, a makkiyya metaphor (where the thing compared is implicit), and then affirms the streams as a takhyīliyya metaphor (where the accessory is explicitly mentioned). This is followed by an elaborate discussion of the metaphor 'qabūl al-qabūl'. The first 'qabūl' means the east wind (al-ṣabā), and the second means acceptance of worship. The author compares the acceptance of worship to the direction from which the favorable wind blows, signifying that divine mercy and forgiveness come from that direction. Then, drawing on Arab beliefs about the east and west winds, he explains how the east wind brings rain and growth, mirroring how acceptance of worship brings spiritual growth and tranquility. This entire discussion serves to illustrate the beauty and richness of Islamic law and worship in a poetic and evocative manner.

Key Terms
شريعة (sharī‘a): The divine law legislated by Allah for His servants, made clear and apparent.
مشارع (mashāri‘): Water channels or streams; used metaphorically for the ways of the sharī‘a from which people derive benefit.
قبول (qabūl): In the first instance: the east wind (al-ṣabā); in the second: acceptance (of worship).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary definition of 'shari‘ah'?
A) A set of water channels
B) The religion legislated by Allah, made clear and apparent
C) The accepted opinions of early scholars
D) A metaphorical garden

Key Takeaway
Sharī‘ah is the divinely legislated path, likened to gardens with flowing streams, from which believers drink mercy and guidance.

The Shar‘ī Definition of Ḥamd: Root of Belief, Branch of Deeds
Defining shar‘ī ḥamd with root and branch analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the shar‘ī definition of ḥamd as taught by the Hanafis and identify its root (aṣl) and branch (far‘) using the Quranic analogy of the good tree.

Core Idea
Shar‘ī ḥamd is a tree: belief is the root, righteous deeds are the branches, and both are essential for acceptance.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawḍīḥ wa Al-Talwīḥ responds to a critical objection: if ḥamd is verbal praise, how can it require belief and action? The answer is that in the sharī‘ah, ḥamd has a broader reality – it includes correct belief (i‘tiqād), spoken praise (qawl), and actions that manifest that belief (a‘māl). The Qur'ānic verse of the good tree (Ibrāhīm 14:24) serves as the proof. Without the root of faith, praise becomes like a bad tree; without the branches of righteous deeds, it bears no fruit and finds no acceptance with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic vs. shar‘ī distinction
Recognize that the objection arises from a purely linguistic view. The passage affirms that ḥamd in the shar‘ī sense is broader, encompassing inner belief and outward actions.
This is a common usul method: expanding the meaning of a term based on revealed texts.
2
Identify the root (aṣl)
The root is correct Islamic belief – firm faith in Allah's perfect attributes, based on the knowledge of tawḥīd and divine attributes, not philosophical theology.
The text explicitly contrasts this with falsafah ilāhiyyāt (philosophical theology).
3
Identify the branch (far‘)
The branch consists of righteous deeds and acts of obedience, performed in accordance with the pure sharī‘ah, based on fiqh.
These deeds are the fruit of faith; without them, the tree is barren.
4
Link to the Quranic simile
The Qur'ān (14:24) compares a good word (kalimah ṭayyibah) to a good tree. The author draws an analogy: ḥamd is a tree whose root is belief and branches are righteous actions.
This dalīl naqlī grounds the definition in revelation.
5
Draw the consequence
If belief is corrupted, the entire ḥamd becomes like a bad tree. If deeds are absent, there is no growth or acceptance (lā namā wa lā qubūl).
This underscores the inseparable link between faith and works in ḥamd.

And al-namā': increase and elevation, meaning growth and rising. It is used both with ya' and waw as weak letters. The true reality of growth is that there is an increase in the dimensions of the body, i.e., length, width, and depth, according to natural proportion. The restriction 'according to natural proportion' is to avoid swelling and disproportionate obesity. Then, because 'al-mahāmid' are intended as the good word, just as in the Noble Qur'an there is: 'Like a good tree, its root is firm and its branch is in the sky' (14:24), similarly, a mahmūda (praiseworthy thing) is a tree that has a root, which is faith and correct beliefs, and a branch, which are good deeds and acts of obedience. And the verification of that is the answer to an objection: Hamd is an act of the tongue; how can faith as a root and acts of obedience as a branch be established in it? Belief and faith are actions of the heart, and deeds are actions of the limbs; they can be included in shukr, but they cannot enter into hamd. The summary of the answer: Even though hamd in its linguistic meaning is only an act of the tongue, in the shar‘ī sense its meaning is broader. As Imam Razi states in his tafsir, 'Mamd (praise) is not merely the statement 'al-ḥamdu lillāh', but rather everything that is concerned with the veneration of Allah and indicates His exaltation and greatness, such as the belief that Allah is described with perfect attributes, then translating that into words and expressions, and actions that indicate that belief – all these things are called hamd.' Therefore, correct belief is the root of this hamd. If the belief is not correct, then the hamd becomes like a bad tree that has no strong root, in the category of 'torn out from above the earth, having no stability' (14:26). And righteous action is the branch. If it is absent, then there is neither growth nor acceptance with Allah; rather it is like a trunk without branches, and like a tree that does not bear fruit. For righteous action is a means to attain gardens and elevation in ranks. 'And righteous action is raised up' – and a hadith states: 'If he has no righteous action, it is not accepted.' The author in his text here indicated that the praiseworthy tree has a root – that is Islamic belief that is firmly established, based on the knowledge of tawḥīd and the divine attributes, not on philosophical theology – and a fruitful branch that is accepted by Allah the Mighty – that is righteous action that conforms to the pure sharī‘ah, based on the science of laws and rulings, i.e., fiqh. In 'ilayhi yaṣ‘ad' the preposition 'ilā' is prefixed to indicate exclusivity, and the imperfect verb 'yaṣ‘ad' indicates continuity and permanence. His statement 'alā an ja‘ala ta‘līq' means the author
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage begins with a definition of 'namā'' (growth) as a natural increase in dimensions proportionally, distinguishing it from abnormal swelling. It then delves into the shar‘ī meaning of hamd, responding to a potential objection: if hamd is merely verbal praise, how can it have a root (faith) and a branch (actions)? The answer is that in the shar‘ī context, hamd has a broader meaning encompassing belief in Allah's attributes, verbal expression, and actions that indicate that belief. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse about the good tree. Correct belief is the root (aṣl), and righteous deeds are the branches (far‘). Without correct belief, hamd is like a bad tree; without righteous deeds, it lacks growth and acceptance. The author then points out that acceptance of deeds requires both correct belief (root) and righteous deeds (branches) in accordance with the sharī‘ah. The final part mentions that the author indicates that the tree of praise has a root (Islamic belief based on tawḥīd and divine attributes, not philosophy) and a branch (righteous actions based on fiqh). The use of 'ilayhi yaṣ‘ad' emphasizes exclusivity and continuity.

Key Terms
نماء (namā’): Natural proportionate growth, as opposed to swelling.
حمد (شرعی) (ḥamd (shar‘ī)): The sharī‘ah reality of hamd, broader than mere verbal praise; includes belief, words, and deeds.
اصل (aṣl): Root; here referring to faith and correct belief as the foundation of hamd.
فرع (far‘): Branch; here referring to righteous deeds that spring from belief.

Key Takeaway
Shar‘ī ḥamd is not limited to verbal praise; it requires correct belief as its root and righteous deeds as its branch.

Expanding Praise: Faith as Root, Deeds as Branches
Commentary on the Quranic metaphor of the good tree applied to praise in Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principles of bayan and takhsis to derive the Sharia definition of hamd from the Quranic metaphor.

Core Idea
Praise in Sharia is not just verbal; it requires faith as root and deeds as branches.

The Quranic verse 'like a good tree' is more than a beautiful simile—it becomes a legal tool for definition. This lesson shows how Hanafi usul uses bayan, qiyas, and takhsis to expand the concept of praise from a mere utterance to a comprehensive act encompassing belief and action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary Quranic dalil
Locate the verse (Ibrahim 14:24) and its key simile: a good tree with firm root and branches in the sky.
This is the foundational text for the analysis.
2
Apply bayan: interpret 'kalimah tayyibah' as 'mahamid'
Using contextual evidence and scholarly consensus, the good word is specified to mean praises (mahamid) in the comprehensive Sharia sense.
Bayan clarifies the ambiguous term.
3
Apply qiyas: map tree structure to hamd components
Root (faith and correct beliefs) and branches (righteous deeds) are analogized to the parts of hamd. This yields the requirement of both for valid praise.
Qiyas here is metaphorical but still uses analogy.
4
Apply takhsis: restrict the general linguistic meaning of hamd
The general term 'hamd' (linguistically only verbal) is restricted by the Sharia evidence to include belief and deeds. This is the final derivation.
Takhsis resolves the objection that hamd is only verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the praises (mahamid) because by 'good word' is meant praises, as in the noble Quran: 'like a good tree whose root is firm and whose branches are in the sky.' Accordingly, the praising (muhammada) is a tree for which there is a root, and that is faith and correct beliefs, and also a branch, and they are deeds and acts of obedience. And the verification of that is an answer to the objection that praise (hamd) is an act of the tongue; how then can faith as root and deeds as branches be established in it? Belief and faith are acts of the heart, and deeds are acts of the limbs; both can be included in gratitude (shukr) but not in praise. The summary of the answer is that although praise linguistically is only an act of the tongue, from the Sharia perspective its meaning is broader than that, as Imam Razi states in his interpretation: 'praise is not only the utterance of 'al-hamdu lillah' by the speaker, but every thing that is associated with the exaltation of Allah and indicates His glorification and greatness, e.g., the belief that Allah is qualified with attributes of perfection, then its translation and expression through speech and words, and the deeds that indicate that belief — all these things are called praise.' Therefore, correct belief is the root of this praise; if belief is not correct, then the praise will be like a bad tree whose root is not firm, being in the category of 'uprooted from above the earth, having no stability.' And righteous deed is the branch; if it is absent, then for him there is neither growth nor acceptance with Allah, but rather it will be like a trunk without twigs, and like a tree without fruit, because righteous deed is the means to attaining gardens and raising ranks. And it is mentioned in hadith: 'When there is no righteous deed for him, it is not accepted.' The author (musannif) pointed in his expression to the fact that the tree of praise has a root, which is Islamic belief firmly rooted, based on the science of tawhid and attributes, not on philosophical theology; and it has a branch that is accepted by Allah, the Almighty, which is righteous deed in accordance with the pure Sharia, based on the science of laws and rulings, i.e., the science of fiqh. In 'ilayhi yas'adu' the introduction of 'ilayhi' indicates specification, and the imperfect verb 'yas'adu' indicates continuity and permanence. His saying 'ala anna ja'ala ta'liq' means the author.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents an analytical expansion of the concept of 'حمد' (praise) in a Sharia context, using the Quranic metaphor of a good tree (Surah Ibrahim 14:24). The author begins by identifying that the 'good word' in the verse refers to praises (محامد). He then constructs a tree analogy where the root is faith (ایمان) and correct beliefs (اعتقادات صحیحہ), and the branches are righteous deeds and acts of obedience (اعمال وطاعات). This is a response to a potential objection: since praise is ordinarily a verbal act, how can non-verbal elements like belief and deeds be included? The answer, citing Imam Razi, is that in Sharia, praise has a broader meaning: it encompasses any statement or action that indicates reverence for Allah. Thus, correct belief is the essential root; without it, praise is like a bad tree with no stability. Righteous deeds are the branches; without them, there is no growth or divine acceptance. The author then emphasizes that the root must be based on sound theology (علم التوحید والصفات) not philosophy, and the deeds must conform to the Sharia, which is the domain of fiqh. The use of 'الیہ یصعد' (to Him ascends) indicates specification and continuity, reinforcing that only the correct belief and deeds are elevated and accepted. The author also notes a hadith about the necessity of righteous deeds for acceptance. This passage is typical of a sharh (commentary) that expands on a matn statement, using textual analysis to derive deeper meanings.

Key Terms
حمد (hamd): In this text, a comprehensive Sharia term that includes verbal praise, belief in Allah's attributes, and righteous deeds that indicate reverence.
محامد (mahamid): Plural of mahmada; objects of praise, identified with the 'good word' in the Quranic verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Sharia definition of praise (hamd) integrates correct belief and righteous deeds, as derived from the Quranic tree simile through Hanafi usul operations.

The Four Pillars of Islamic Law and Their Hierarchical Order
Commentary on the fortress simile and the order of the four sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four sources of Islamic law according to the Hanafi school, state their relative order, and explain the condition that affects the precedence of Sunnah over Ijma.

Core Idea
The Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas form the fortress of rulings; Sunnah's text is prior to Ijma only when definitive.

This passage from a classical Hanafi usul commentary explains the famous simile of legal rulings as a fortress built on four pillars. These pillars are the foundational sources of Sharia: the Quran, the Sunnah, Ijma (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy). Understanding their precise order and the condition governing the precedence of Sunnah over Ijma is crucial for proper legal derivation in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four pillars
List the four sources mentioned: the Book (Quran), the Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas. Note that the author mentions them in that order.
2
Understand the fortress simile
Explain how the legal rulings protect believers from sin and punishment, similar to a fortress protecting from enemies and elements.
3
Analyze the order of mention
The commentator states the order explicitly: Book first, then Sunnah, then Ijma, and finally Qiyas (mentioned implicitly). Recognize that this reflects their rank in the Sharia.
4
Recognize the condition on Sunnah's precedence
The passage clarifies that Sunnah's precedence over Ijma is absolute only when the Sunnah text (matn) is definitive (qat'i) in transmission. If its transmission is speculative (zanni), it loses that status and Ijma may take precedence.
This is a distinctively Hanafi nuance.
5
Apply the rule
Given a hypothetical scenario, determine whether a given Sunnah is qat'i or zanni, and then decide its order relative to Ijma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور علم الکلام وہ علم ہے جو احوال صانع اور احوال نبوت و امامت ومعاد وغیرہ سے بحث کرتا ہے علی طریقہ قانون الاسلام على طريقة الحكمة الالهية الفلسفية بنى على اربعة اركان یہ جملہ اپنے سابقہ جملہ سے بمنزلہ بدل اشتمال کے ہے کیونکہ جملہ سابقہ یعنی جعل اصول الشریعہ اخذ کی بنسبت عظمت علم اور اس کی جلالت قدر کو بیان کرنے میں اونی ہے جیسے کہ بدل او فی ابتبیین المراد ہوتا ہے بنسبت مبدل منہ کے اور حقیقی بدل نہیں کیونکہ مبدل منہ مقصود بالنسیتہ نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں ایسے نہیں بلکہ جملہ سابقہ بھی مقصود ہے خلاصہ اس جملہ کا یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے احکام کے قصر کو اربعہ ارکان پر مبنی فرمایا اور آیات محکمات کے ساتھ اسے انتہائی مستحکم اور مضبوط بنا دیا اور آیات متشابہات کو استار کے خیموں میں مقصور اور محبوس کر دیا تا کہ علماء راسخین کی ابتلاء و آزمائش ہو جیسے کہ مختار عند الاحناف ہے کہ وَمَا لَ لَم تَأْوِيلَهُ إِلَّا الله پر وقف نام ہے یعنی ان متشابہات کی تاویل و تفسیر صرف اللہ تعالیٰ ہی کو معلوم ہے علماء راسخین کا کام یہ ہے کہ اپنے اذہان کی باگ وڈور کو یہاں تھام لیں اور جو علم بالاسرار ان کا محبوب اور قابل اشتیاق مشغلہ ہے اس تک پہنچنے کی کوشش ترک کر دیں اللہ تعالیٰ نے ان متشابہات پر کسی کو مطلع نہیں فرمایا یہ تو ہے تو ضیح متن اب شرح کی طرف توجہ کیجیے ۔ شبه الاحكام الشرعية یعنی مصنف نے احکام شرعیہ کو قصر کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے جس طرح قصر میں جگہ پکڑ کر گرمی سردی سے بچاؤ حاصل کیا جاتا ہے اور اعداء کے خطرات سے اطمینان قصر قلعہ میں داخل ہو کر حاصل کیا جاتا ہے اسی طرح ان احکام شرعیہ کی طرف التجا اور پناہ حاصل کرنے کے ساتھ عدود دین یعنی شیطان کے مہلکات سے پناہ حاصل ہوتی ہے اور جہنم کے عذاب کی گرمی و سردی سے بچاؤ حاصل کرنا ان احکام شرعیہ پر عمل کرنے سے حاصل ہوتا ہے قصر الاحکام میں مشبہ بہ کی اضافت الی الشیہ ہے جیسے لجین الماء میں لجین یعنی فضہ ہے یعنی پانی جو صفائی میں چاندی کی طرح ہے ۔ والاحکام تستند یہاں قصرا حکام کا احتیاج الی اربعہ ارکان کو ثابت کر رہا ہے یعنی احکام شرعی کے ادلہ جزئیہ کثيرة التعداد ہونے کے باوجود ان کا رجوع اربعہ ارکان کی طرف ہے جو قصر احکام کے ارکان ہیں مقصود ان اربعہ ارکان کی طرف شدہ احتیاج کو ثابت کرنا ہے جیسے قصر مکانی ارکان کی طرف محتاج ہے اگرچہ ارکان قصر مکانی کے داخلی اجزاء ہیں اور قصر احکام میں یہ اربعہ ارکان داخل تو نہیں ہیں لیکن ان کی طرف احتیاج ایسے ہے جیسے احتیاج کل الی الجز ہوتا ہے۔ فذكر في اثناء الكلام پھر مصنف نے اللہ کلام میں ان زریعہ ارکان کا ذکرہ اسی ترتیب سے کیا جس طرح شارع نے احکام کی بنیاد ان یہ رکھی تھی کتاب کو پہلے ذکر کیا پھر سنت پھر اجماع اس کے بعد عمل بالقیات کا ذکر اشارہ کیا کہ قیاس کے موافق عمل ہوتا ہے نہ کہ اعتقاد چونکہ پہلے تین اصول مطلقہ کا ملہ تھے اس لئے ان کا ذکر صراحہ کیا اور قیاس چونکہ اصالہ میں اُن کی بنسبت کم درجہ تھا اس لئے اس کا ذکر ضمنا کیا بقولہ وضع معالم العلم على مسالك المعتبرین ای القاسین یعنی جو حضرات نصوص اور علل احکام میں غور و فکر کرنے على کے بعد قیاس کرتے ہیں فاعتبروا یا اولی الابصار سے لفظ معتبرین اخذ کیا جیسے اعتبرت الشی اس وقت کہا جاتا ہے جب اس شی کی طرف پوری نظر فکر کر کے اس کے حالات کی رعایت اور نگہداشت کریں ۔ والمعلم یعنی معلم اس اثر و نشان کو کہتے جس سے طریق و راستہ پر دلیل کا کام لیا جاتا ہے مراد اس سے علیہ حکم ہے جو مقیس علیہ میں ہوتی ہے جس سے مقیس میں ثبوت حکم پر استدلال کیا جاتا ہے۔ فان قلت خلاصہ سوال یہ ہے کہ ان اربعہ ارکان کی ترتیب مذکورہ علی الاطلاق تو نہیں ہے کیونکہ سنت کی تقدیم علی الاجماع اس وقت ہوئی جب وہ قطعی ہو جب ظنی ہو تو پھرا جماع پر مقدم نہیں ہے۔ قلت خلاصہ جواب یہ ہے کہ کلام مذکورہ متن سنت کے متعلق ہے اور متن سنت على الاطلاق مقدم على الاجماع ہے اور جہاں مؤخر عن الاجماع ہوتی ہے وہاں اس کا ثبوت مظنون ہو جاتا اس کا سنت ہونا مشکوک ہو جاتا ہے۔
And the science of kalam is the science that discusses the states of the Creator and the states of prophethood, imamate, resurrection, etc., according to the method of the Islamic law (Qanun al-Islam) on the philosophical divine method. It is built upon four pillars. This sentence is in the position of a substitutive apposition (badal ishtimal) to its preceding sentence, because the preceding sentence, i.e., 'making the principles of Sharia taken' is more comprehensive in terms of the greatness of the science and its majestic value, just as a substitution clarifies the intended meaning relative to the substitute. And it is not a true substitution because the substitute is not intended in itself, and here it is not like that; rather, the preceding sentence is also intended. The summary of this sentence is that Allah the Exalted based the fortress of rulings on four pillars, and with the clear verses (ayat muhkamat) He made it extremely firm and strong, and confined and imprisoned the ambiguous verses (ayat mutashabihat) under the tents of concealment, so that the deeply rooted scholars may be tested, as is the chosen position of the Hanafis that the stopping (waqf) is on 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah' (Quran 3:7), meaning that the interpretation and explanation of these ambiguous verses is known only to Allah. The duty of the deeply rooted scholars is to rein in their minds here and abandon the effort to attain the knowledge of secrets, which is their beloved and desirable occupation. Allah has not informed anyone about these ambiguous verses. This is the text of Tawdih; now turn to the commentary. 'Shabbaha al-ahkam al-shar'iyya' means the author likened the legal rulings to a fortress. Just as in a fortress one protects oneself from heat and cold by taking shelter, and gains security from the dangers of enemies by entering into a fortified castle, similarly, by seeking refuge and shelter in these legal rulings, one gains protection from the destructive forces of the religion, i.e., the deadly traps of Satan, and protection from the heat and cold of the punishment of Hell is achieved by acting upon these legal rulings. In 'qasr al-ahkam', the addition of the simile is like 'lujayn al-ma' where 'lujayn' means silver, i.e., water that is like silver in purity. 'Wa al-ahkam tastanid' here proves that the fortress of rulings has need of four pillars, meaning that even though the particular evidences of legal rulings are numerous, they all return to the four pillars which are the pillars of the fortress of rulings. The intent is to prove the intense need for these four pillars, just as a spatial fortress is in need of pillars, even though the pillars of a spatial fortress are its internal parts, while in the fortress of rulings these four pillars are not internal parts, but the need for them is like the need of a whole for its part. Then he mentioned in the midst of the speech: then the author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned these pillars in the same order that the Lawgiver based the rulings upon them: he mentioned the Book first, then the Sunnah, then Ijma, and then after that, action by Qiyas. He indicated that action is in accordance with Qiyas, not belief. Since the first three are absolute principles, he mentioned them explicitly, and since Qiyas is lower in rank than them as a principle, he mentioned it implicitly by his statement: 'He placed the landmarks of knowledge on the paths of the considerers (al-mu'tabireen)', i.e., those who reflect on the texts and the effective causes of rulings and then perform Qiyas. The word 'mu'tabireen' is taken from 'fa'tabiru ya uli al-absar' (Quran 59:2). Just as you say 'i'tabartu al-shay' when you look at something with full consideration and care for its states. 'Al-mu'allim' means the sign or marker that serves as a proof for the path. What is meant by it is the 'illah (effective cause) of the ruling that exists in the analogized case (maqees alayh), by which the establishment of the ruling in the new case (maqees) is argued. Then it is said: summary of the question: This order of the four pillars is not absolute, because the precedence of Sunnah over Ijma occurs when it is definitive (qat'i); when it is speculative (zanni), then it is not precedent over Ijma. Answer: summary of the response: The speech concerns the text (matn) of the Sunnah, and the text of the Sunnah is absolutely precedent over Ijma. And where it is postponed after Ijma, there its proof becomes speculative, and its being Sunnah becomes doubtful.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by the author of Altaleequlfaseeh (the gloss on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih). It opens by contextualizing the science of kalam as built on four pillars, then explains the simile of rulings as a fortress (qasr) that protects from evil. The commentator clarifies that the four pillars are the foundational sources: the Book (Quran), the Sunnah, Ijma (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy). He emphasizes that all particular evidences revert to these pillars. The order of mention—Kitab first, then Sunnah, then Ijma, then Qiyas—reflects their rank in the Sharia. Qiyas is mentioned implicitly because it is a derivative source. A question arises: is Sunnah always before Ijma? The answer explains that the text (matn) of Sunnah is always prior, but if a transmission is speculative, it loses the status of Sunnah in that context. This is a nuanced Hanafi position that distinguishes between the definitive and speculative transmission of Sunnah.

Key Terms
قصر الأحكام (qasr al-ahkam): The fortress of rulings; a metaphor for the protective structure of Islamic law that safeguards believers from sin and punishment.
متن السنة (matn al-sunnah): The text of the Sunnah (whether from the Prophet's sayings, actions, or tacit approvals), considered definitive in its precedence over Ijma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view expressed in this passage, when can Sunnah be considered subordinate to Ijma?
A) Never; Sunnah is always precedent.
B) When the Sunnah is transmitted through a zanni (speculative) chain.
C) When Ijma is based on explicit Quranic text.
D) When Qiyas contradicts the Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The four sources of Islamic law are Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas, but Sunnah's absolute precedence over Ijma is contingent on its transmission being definitive (qat'i); otherwise, Ijma takes precedence.

Categories of Quranic Verses and the Hanafi View on Mutashabih
Commentary on types of Quranic verses: muhkam, nass, mutashabih, mujmal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four categories of Quranic verses (muhkam, nass, mutashabih, mujmal) and state the Hanafi position that the interpretation of mutashabih is known only to Allah.

Core Idea
Only Allah knows the true interpretation of mutashabih; scholars are tested by being prevented from seeking it.

This passage classifies Quranic verses by their clarity using a fortress analogy: some rulings are fully manifest (muhkam), others clear but less so (nass), some extremely hidden (mutashabih), and others hidden but accessible through effort (mujmal). The Hanafi school emphasizes that mutashabih verses are completely concealed, and their interpretation is known solely to Allah. The wisdom behind revealing such verses is to test the deeply rooted scholars by barring them from the knowledge they desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories and their definitions
List muhkam, nass, mutashabih, and mujmal. Note their degrees of clarity: muhkam is fully manifest, nass is clear, mutashabih is extremely hidden, mujmal is hidden but discoverable with effort.
This classification is based on the fortress analogy in the text.
2
Understand the fortress analogy
The author likens rulings in the Book to items in a spatial fortress: some are fully visible, some partially, some completely hidden (only the lord knows), and some hidden but reachable with effort.
The earlier comparison of rulings to a fortress is distinct from the Book itself; the fortress analogy here applies to the types of verses.
3
Distinguish the Hanafi position on mutashabih
The Hanafi school holds that the interpretation of mutashabih is known only to Allah, and scholars must refrain from seeking it. This is in contrast to some other schools that allow qualified scholars to interpret.
The passage describes the tents of concealment completely covering mutashabih.
4
Recognize the purpose of mutashabih
The benefit of revealing mutashabih is to test the deeply rooted scholars by preventing them from attaining the knowledge they desire, akin to being tested by being barred from one’s wishes.
This is drawn directly from the passage: 'فائدہ انزاله… راسخین فی العلم کا ابتلاء'.

Source Text

ثم ذكر بعض اقسام الكتاب یعنی جیسے قصر مکانی جو بدرجہ مشبہ یہ ہے اس میں بعض اشیاء انتہائی ظہور و اظہار کے درجہ میں ہوتی ہیں اور بعض کم درجہ ظاہر ہوتی ہیں اسی طرح بعض اشیاء انتہائی خفی ہوتی ہیں جو صرف رب القصر ان اللہ تک پہنچ سکتا ہے اس کے علاوہ اور کسی کی رسائی نہیں ہوتی اور بعض اشیاء اتنا خفا میں نہیں ہوتی دوسرے اشخاص بھی کوشش کے ساتھ پہنچ سکتے ہیں اسی طرح کتاب اللہ العزیز میں بعض احکام ظہور کے درجہ اعلیٰ میں ہیں جیسے محکم بعض اس سے کم درجہ میں جیسے نص بعض انتہائی اففی ہیں جو صرف باری تعالیٰ کے علم میں ہیں جیسے متشابہ اور بعض اس سے کم درجہ کے خفی ہیں کوشش اور جدو جہد کے ساتھ پہنچا جا سکتا ہے جیسے مجمل یہ کتاب اللہ العزیز کے اقسام ہیں اور قصر احکام کی اشیا نہیں ہے اور ماقبل میں احکام کو تشبیہ بالقصر دی تھی نہ کہ کتاب کو پس یہ توجیہ ماقبل کے منافی ہے۔ فتنبہ ۔ قق له مقصورات ای محبوسات جعل مصنف خیام استتار کو متشابہ پر ایسا مضروب اور محیط قرار دیا ہے جس سے متشابہ کے بدو اور ظہور کی رجاء و امید ختم ہے جیسے کہ مذہب احناف ہے کہ متشابہ کی تاویل و تفسیر صرف اللہ تعالیٰ کے علم میں ہے اور کسی پر اس کا ظہور نہیں ہے ۔ فائدہ انزاله اس کے انزال کا فائدہ صرف میں ہے کہ راسخین فی العلم کا ابتلاء اور آزمائش ہو کہ انہیں اس کے اندر غور و فکر کرنے سے منع کیا جائے اور ان کی جو متمنا اور آرزو ہے کہ علم تجمیع الاسرار حاصل ہو جائے اس تک پہنچنے سے روک دیا جائے کیونکہ ہر ایک کو اپنے مطلوب اور متمنا سے روک کر آزمائش کی جاتی ہے جیسے جہاں کے
Then he mentioned some categories of the Book. For example, just as a spatial fortress has some things in the degree of extreme manifestation and clarity, and some are less manifest; similarly, some things are extremely hidden, which only the lord of the fortress can reach, with no access for anyone else, and some things are not so hidden—other persons can reach them with effort. Likewise, in the Mighty Book of Allah, some rulings are at the highest degree of clarity, like muhkam; some are at a lower degree, like nass; some are extremely hidden, which are only in the knowledge of Allah the Exalted, like mutashabih; and some are hidden to a lesser degree, and can be reached with effort and struggle, like mujmal. These are the categories of the Mighty Book of Allah, not the things of the fortress of rulings. And earlier, rulings were likened to a fortress, not the Book. So this interpretation contradicts the earlier. So be warned. 'Qq lahu maqsuratun', i.e., confined. The author made the tents of concealment as such a covering and encompassing over the mutashabih that hope for its emergence and manifestation is cut off, as per the Hanafi school that the interpretation and explanation of mutashabih is only in the knowledge of Allah the Exalted, and its manifestation is not to anyone. The benefit of its revelation is only that the deeply rooted scholars be tested, that they be prevented from delving into it and be stopped from attaining their wish and desire to acquire the knowledge of the collection of secrets, because everyone is tested by being prevented from their desired and wished thing, just as in the Hereafter...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on Tawdih, now discussing the categories of Quranic verses. The commentator draws an analogy with a physical fortress where items have varying degrees of visibility. Similarly, Quranic verses (ahkam) have different levels of clarity: muhkam (most clear), nass (clear but with some nuance), mutashabih (extremely hidden, known only to Allah), and mujmal (hidden but can be understood through effort). The commentator notes a potential confusion: earlier rulings were likened to a fortress, not the Book itself, so this categorization of verses is correct. He then emphasizes the Hanafi position on mutashabih: its interpretation is exclusively known to Allah, and the tents of concealment completely cover it. The purpose of revealing mutashabih is to test the deeply rooted scholars by preventing them from seeking interpretation, as they are tested by being barred from their desires.

Key Terms
محكم (muhkam): A verse whose meaning is clear and unambiguous; highest degree of clarity.
نص (nass): A verse that is clear but with some nuance; lower clarity than muhkam.
متشابه (mutashabih): A verse whose meaning is hidden and known only to Allah; concealed by tents of secrecy.
مجمل (mujmal): A verse that is hidden to a lesser degree; can be understood through effort and research.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the purpose of revealing mutashabih verses?
A) To provide a legal ruling that applies universally.
B) To test the deeply rooted scholars by preventing them from seeking interpretation.
C) To allow scholars to demonstrate their interpretive skills.
D) To be used as evidence for qiyas.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses have varying levels of clarity; mutashabih is completely hidden, and its interpretation is known only to Allah, serving as a test for scholars.

Al-Quran as Source

The Four Categories of Quranic Clarity: Muhkam, Nass, Mujmal, Mutashabih
Classification of Quranic verses into clarity levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Quranic verses into muhkam, nass, mutashabih, and mujmal, and explain the Hanafi position that mutashabih is known only to Allah.

Core Idea
Quranic verses vary in clarity: muhkam and nass are clear; mujmal can be clarified with effort; mutashabih is known only to Allah.

Before deriving rulings from the Quran, a Hanafi usuli must classify each verse by its level of clarity. This passage presents a four-fold classification using the analogy of a palace with rooms of varying visibility. Understanding these categories is essential for proper istinbat and for recognizing the limits of human interpretation in certain verses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Palace Analogy
Just as a palace has rooms of different degrees of visibility (some fully open, some hidden), the Quran has verses of varying clarity.
The analogy helps internalize that clarity is a spectrum, not binary.
2
Identify the Four Categories in Order
From clearest to most obscure: muhkam (self-evident), nass (clear but less so), mujmal (obscure but attainable via effort), mutashabih (extremely hidden, known only to Allah).
Memorize the order: muhkam > nass > mujmal > mutashabih in clarity.
3
Distinguish Mujmal from Mutashabih
Mujmal can be understood through exertion and external evidence (e.g., Sunnah, context); mutashabih cannot be understood by any human effort.
This distinction is crucial: mujmal is open to tafsir, mutashabih is closed.
4
Learn the Hanafi Position on Mutashabih
The Hanafi school holds that the true interpretation of mutashabih verses is known only to Allah (waqf). Scholars are prohibited from attempting to interpret them.
This contradicts the view of some other schools who allow interpretation by the firmly grounded in knowledge.
5
Reflect on the Benefit (Fā'idah)
The revelation of mutashabih serves as a test for the scholars: they are prevented from attaining what they desire most (knowledge of secrets) as a trial of their obedience.
This shows that not all divine communication is meant to be fully understood by humans.

Source Text

ثم ذكر بعض اقسام الكتاب یعنی جیسے قصر مکانی جو بدرجہ مشبہ یہ ہے اس میں بعض اشیاء انتہائی ظہور و اظہار کے درجہ میں ہوتی ہیں اور بعض کم درجہ ظاہر ہوتی ہیں اسی طرح بعض اشیاء انتہائی خفی ہوتی ہیں جو صرف رب القصر ان اللہ تک پہنچ سکتا ہے اس کے علاوہ اور کسی کی رسائی نہیں ہوتی اور بعض اشیاء اتنا خفا میں نہیں ہوتی دوسرے اشخاص بھی کوشش کے ساتھ پہنچ سکتے ہیں اسی طرح کتاب اللہ العزیز میں بعض احکام ظہور کے درجہ اعلیٰ میں ہیں جیسے محکم بعض اس سے کم درجہ میں جیسے نص بعض انتہائی اففی ہیں جو صرف باری تعالیٰ کے علم میں ہیں جیسے متشابہ اور بعض اس سے کم درجہ کے خفی ہیں کوشش اور جدو جہد کے ساتھ پہنچا جا سکتا ہے جیسے مجمل یہ کتاب اللہ العزیز کے اقسام ہیں اور قصر احکام کی اشیا نہیں ہے اور ماقبل میں احکام کو تشبیہ بالقصر دی تھی نہ کہ کتاب کو پس یہ توجیہ ماقبل کے منافی ہے۔ فتنبہ ۔ قق له مقصورات ای محبوسات جعل مصنف خیام استتار کو متشابہ پر ایسا مضروب اور محیط قرار دیا ہے جس سے متشابہ کے بدو اور ظہور کی رجاء و امید ختم ہے جیسے کہ مذہب احناف ہے کہ متشابہ کی تاویل و تفسیر صرف اللہ تعالیٰ کے علم میں ہے اور کسی پر اس کا ظہور نہیں ہے ۔ فائدہ انزاله اس کے انزال کا فائدہ صرف میں ہے کہ راسخین فی العلم کا ابتلاء اور آزمائش ہو کہ انہیں اس کے اندر غور و فکر کرنے سے منع کیا جائے اور ان کی جو متمنا اور آرزو ہے کہ علم تجمیع الاسرار حاصل ہو جائے اس تک پہنچنے سے روک دیا جائے کیونکہ ہر ایک کو اپنے مطلوب اور متمنا سے روک کر آزمائش کی جاتی ہے جیسے جہاں کے نزدیک علم غیر مطلوب اور غیر مقصود ہے ان کی آزمائش تحصیل علم کے حکم سے کی جاتی ہے اور علماء کے نزدیک چونکہ علم بالاسرار مطلوب و محبوب ہے اس لئے ان کی آزمائش اس حکم کے ساتھ کی گئی ہے کہ اسے ترک کر دو اور طلب نہ کرو
Then he mentioned some divisions of the Book: just as in spatial qasr (shortening) which is by way of example, in it some things are of the highest degree of manifestation, and some are of a lesser degree of manifestation; similarly some things are extremely hidden, which only the Lord of the qasr—Allah—can reach, no one else can access; and some things are not so hidden, other persons can reach them with effort. Similarly, in the Noble Book of Allah, some rulings are of the highest degree of manifestation, like muhkam; some are of a lesser degree, like nass; some are extremely hidden, which are only in the knowledge of Allah Almighty, like mutashābih; and some are of a lesser degree of hiddenness, can be accessed with effort and exertion, like mujmal. These are the divisions of the Noble Book of Allah, and they are not the things of the qasr (shortening) of rulings. And previously, rulings were compared to qasr, not the Book, so this explanation contradicts what preceded. So pay attention. He said: maqṣūrāt i.e., confined. The author made the tents of concealment placed over mutashābih such that all hope of its appearance and manifestation is cut off, just as the Hanafi school holds that the interpretation and explanation of mutashābih is only in the knowledge of Allah, and its manifestation is not upon anyone. Benefit of its revelation: The benefit of its revelation is only that the firmly grounded in knowledge be tested, that they be prevented from pondering over it, and that they be prevented from attaining what they desire and wish, that is obtaining the knowledge of the accumulation of secrets, because everyone is tested by being prevented from what they seek and desire. Similarly, for those who consider knowledge of secrets as undesired and unintended, their test is by the command to seek knowledge; and for scholars, since knowledge of secrets is desired and beloved, their test is by the command to abandon it and not seek it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis classifying the verses of the Quran into four categories by clarity. The commentator uses the analogy of a spatial qasr (palace) to illustrate varying degrees of manifestness (ẓuhūr) and hiddenness (khafā). The categories are: (1) muhkam: the clearest, self-evident in meaning; (2) nass: clear but slightly less than muhkam; (3) mutashābih: extremely hidden, known only to Allah—this is the Hanafi view, that its true interpretation is solely with Allah, a stance differing from some other schools; (4) mujmal: obscure but attainable through effort and academic exertion. The passage clarifies that this classification pertains to the Book itself, not to rulings (aḥkām). It notes that earlier the author had compared rulings to qasr, but here it is about the Book, so the earlier comparison was for a different point. The 'benefit' (fā'idah) of revealing mutashābihāt is to test the scholars, forbidding them from pursuing what they most desire (the hidden meanings), as a trial. This contrasts with the test of those who dislike such knowledge.

Key Terms
محکم (muḥkam): The clearest type of verse, self-evident in meaning.
نص (naṣṣ): Clear, but slightly less evident than muḥkam.
متشابہ (mutashābih): Extremely hidden; its meaning known only to Allah.
مجمل (mujmal): Obscure but attainable through effort and exertion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which category of Quranic verse can be understood through effort?
A) Muḥkam
B) Naṣṣ
C) Mutashābih
D) Mujmal

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that mutashābih verses are only known to Allah and cannot be interpreted by humans, unlike mujmal which can be clarified through effort.

Al-Quran as Source

Attributing Meanings to the Quran: Divine Intention or Ijtihad?
Discusses the dual attribution of Quranic meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that Quranic interpretations derived through ijtihad are valid and can be attributed to the scholars while the Quran's meaning remains divinely intended.

Core Idea
Quranic meanings are divinely intended, but their extraction by qualified scholars through ijtihad is a valid attribution to human reasoning.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text addresses a foundational question: are the meanings we derive from the Quran directly from Allah, or are they the result of scholars' reasoning? Using a famous incident involving Caliph Umar and Ibn Abbas, the author demonstrates that both perspectives can be simultaneously true. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its significance for the authority of ijtihad in tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Claim
Read the passage carefully and identify the author's main assertion about how Quranic meanings can be described. The passage states that meanings can be attributed both to Allah ('Malik al-Haqq al-Mubin') and to the thoughts of thinkers ('afkar al-mutafakkirin').
2
Deconstruct the Example
Analyze the story of Umar and Ibn Abbas. Note that Umar asked about the interpretation of 'Idha ja'a nasrullahi wal-fath'. The Companions gave various answers, and Ibn Abbas offered a unique interpretation indicating the Prophet's impending death. Umar approved it.
The hadith is cited as an example of ijtihad in tafsir.
3
Connect to Usul Principle
Understand how this story supports the Hanafi view that ijtihad (reasoning) is a valid method for interpreting the Quran. The approval of a Companion (Umar) indicates that such interpretation is acceptable and can be considered both a divine meaning (since it explains the Quran) and a product of human thought (since it came through Ibn Abbas' reasoning).
In Hanafi usul, the practice of the Companions (qawl al-sahabi) is a source of law.
4
Evaluate the Implication
Reflect on the broader implication: this principle allows for multiple valid interpretations without diminishing the divine origin of the Quran. It also justifies the use of qiyas and istihsan in deriving rulings from the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جیسے ملک الحق المبین کی طرف منسوب کیا جا سکتا ہے اسی طرح افکار متفکرین کے نتائج بھی قرار دیا جا سکتا ہے جیسے کہ مروی ہے کہ حضرت عمر رضی اللہ عنہ نے مجلس صحابہ میں جس کے اندر حضرت ابن عباس رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہ بھی موجود تھے تو اِذَا جَاء نصر الله والفتح کی تفسیر پوچھی تو صحابہ نے مختلف تفسیریں ذکر کیں تو حضرت عمر رضی اللہ عنہ نے ابن عباس رضی اللہ عنہ سے مخاطب ہو کر کہا کہ کیا آپ کی رائے بھی ایسے ہے تو ابن عباس رضی اللہ عنہ نے کہا نہیں بل ہو قرب اجل الرسول علیہ الصلوة والسلام و استحسنه عمر رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہ ۔ تو اس قسم کے مفہوم و معنیٰ کو جیسے ملک الحق المبین کی طرف منسوب کیا جا سکتا ہے اسی طرح نتائج و افکار المتفکرین بھی قرار دیا جا سکتا ہے لاتنا فی بینا۔
Just as it can be attributed to the King of Truth, the Manifest, similarly it can be considered the results of the thoughts of thinkers. For example, it is narrated that Hadrat Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) asked in the assembly of the Companions, which included Hadrat Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him), about the interpretation of 'When the help of Allah and the victory come', so the Companions mentioned various interpretations. Then Hadrat Umar addressed Ibn Abbas saying: 'Is your opinion also like this?' Ibn Abbas said: 'No, rather it is the nearness of the death of the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him)' and Umar approved it. So this type of meaning, just as it can be attributed to the King of Truth, the Manifest, similarly it can be considered the results and thoughts of the thinkers, no doubt in it.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of Quranic interpretation. The author uses the story of Umar and Ibn Abbas to show that meanings derived from the Quran can either be considered as divinely intended (attributed to Allah) or as the product of scholars' ijtihad. In the story, Umar asked about the interpretation of Surah al-Nasr. Various Companions gave different interpretations, and Ibn Abbas gave a unique one indicating the Prophet's impending death. Umar approved it. This illustrates that while the Quran's meanings are from Allah, the understanding and extraction of those meanings by qualified scholars is also valid and can be attributed to the thinkers. This is an important usul principle regarding the authority of tafsir and ijtihad.

Key Terms
ملک الحق المبین (Malik al-Haqq al-Mubin): A name for Allah meaning 'the King of Truth, the Manifest'.
افکار متفکرین (Afkar al-Mutafakkirin): The thoughts or opinions of thinkers (scholars) arrived at through ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ibn Abbas say about the meaning of 'When the help of Allah and the victory come'?
A) It indicates the conquest of Mecca.
B) It indicates the nearness of the Prophet's death.
C) It indicates the end of revelations.
D) It indicates the beginning of the Islamic state.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that Quranic interpretations derived through ijtihad are valid and can be attributed to both Allah (as the ultimate source) and the scholar (as the product of reasoning).

The Definition of I'jāz (Inimitability) of the Qur'an
Defining i'jāz and discussing various opinions on the Qur'an's miraculous nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define i'jāz in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from related concepts like balāghah and sihr.

Core Idea
I'jāz means speech that cannot be challenged, not merely the most eloquent expression.

This lesson unpacks the precise definition of i'jāz (inimitability) as applied to the Qur'an. The commentator corrects a common misunderstanding that i'jāz is simply the highest eloquence, showing that it fundamentally means the quality of being unchallengeable. You will explore several opinions on the cause of the Qur'an's miracle, learn why some are rejected, and grasp the linguistic nuance that distinguishes i'jāz from related concepts like sihr (magic).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage discusses the term i'jāz. Read the commentator's correction of the author's initial definition. Note that i'jāz is derived from 'a'jiztuhu' (made incapable).
Focus on the shift from 'most eloquent' to 'unchallengeable'.
2
Understand the commentator's correction
The commentator states that the author's definition (expressing meaning in the most eloquent way) is not the full definition of i'jāz al-kalam. Instead, i'jāz is when speech reaches a level where it cannot be challenged and its like cannot be produced.
This is crucial: i'jāz is about impossibility of imitation, not just eloquence.
3
Review the competing views on the cause of i'jāz
Four views are presented: (1) eloquence (balāghah), (2) reports of the unseen (akhbār al-ghayb), (3) unique style (uslūb gharīb), (4) divine diversion (sarf). Read each briefly.
Note that all agree the Qur'an is miraculous; the dispute is about the cause.
4
Analyze the refutations of the rejected views
The text rejects views (2) and (4). For (2): it would imply that suras without ghayb reports are not miraculous. For (4): it implies that any speech, even lowly, would be more miraculous if challengers are diverted.
Understand the logical structure of each refutation (reductio ad absurdum).
5
Grasp the contrast with sihr and the singular/plural usage
The phrase 'urwat al-i'jāz' uses the singular 'urwa because i'jāz is a single path (it must surpass all others). In contrast, sihr (magic) uses the plural 'ahdāb because it can have multiple ways and degrees.
This linguistic choice reinforces that i'jāz is a unitary quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لان الاعجاز ان مصنف نے اعجاز کی تفسیر کرتے ہوئے کہا ان بودی المعنیٰ یعنی معنی کو ایسے طریقہ پر ادا کیا جائے جو تمام طرق ادا سے ابلغ اور زیادہ بلیغ ہو شارح نے اس کے متعلق کہا لیس تفسیر المفهوم العجاز الكلام یعنی یہ اعجاز کلامی کے مفہوم کی تفسیر نہیں ہے کیونکہ اعجاز کے اندر یہ ضروری نہیں ہے کہ وہ بلاغت کے ساتھ ہو بلکہ اعجاز فی الکلام اس کو کہتے ہیں کہ کلام ایسے مرتبہ پر پہنچ جائے کہ اس کا معارضہ کرنا ممکن نہ ہو اور اس کی مثل پیش کرنا طاقت بشر سے خارج ہو جائے یہ اعجزتہ سے ماخوذ ہے جس کا معنی ہے عاجز کر دیتا اب یہ ضروری نہیں ہے کہ اس اعجاز کا سبب بلاغت ہو بلکہ مختلف امور سبب اعجاز ہو سکتے ہیں یہی وجہ ہے کہ قرآن مجید کے معجز ہونے میں مختلف وجوہ پیش کئے جاتے ہیں باوجود اس کے کہ معجز ہونے پر اتفاق ہے ۔ فقیل انه ببلاغته یعنی قرآن مجید کا معجز ہونا اس وجہ سے ہے کہ وہ بلاغت کے اونچے مقام پر ہے جہاں قوت انسانی کا پہنچنا ناممکن ہے۔ وقیل باخبارہ یعنی بعض کا قول یہ ہے کہ چونکہ قرآن مجید اخبار عن المغیبات پر مشتمل ہے اس لئے معجز ہے کیونکہ انسان اخبار عن المغیبات کی طاقت نہیں رکھتا یہ اس کی حدود علمی سے بالکل خارج ہے۔ اس وجہ کو رد کر دیا گیا ہے کہ جو سورۃ اخبار عن المغیبات پر مشتمل نہ ہو وہ معجز نہ ہو حالانکہ یہ غلط ہے بلکہ ہر سورۃ بلکہ مقدار سورۃ معجز ہے اگر چہ اخبار عن المغیبات پر مشتمل نہ ہو وقیل باسلوبه الغریب یعنی اس کا اسلوب غریب عجیب سے فصحار و بلغار کے اسلوب نثر و نظم دونوں سے مختلف ہے اور اس اسلوب قرآنی سے وہ لوگ نا آشنا تھے وقیل بصورت اللہ تعالیٰ یعنی اللہ تعالیٰ نے ان معارضین کے عقول کو اپنی قوۃ قاہرہ کے ساتھ اس کے معارضہ سے اور اس کے مثل لانے سے پھیر دیا اور دوسری طرف موڑ دیا اس کے معارضہ کی طرف بالکل آنے نہیں دیا یہ وجہ بھی مردود ہے کیونکہ اس سے یہ لازم آتا ہے جو کلام بلاغت کے بالکل نچلے درجہ میں ہو اور معارضہ سے صرف عقول کر دیا جائے تو اس کا اعجاز قوی تر ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ با وجود ممکن المعارضہ ہونے کے اس کا معارضہ نہ ہو سکے یہ صرف العقول میں اظہر ہے اور اعجاز میں اقوئی ہونی چاہیئے ۔ بل المراد یعنی مصنف کی مراد قرآن مجید کے اعجاز کے متعلق جو صحیح رہی ہے اس کو پیش کرنا ہے یعنی قرآن مجید کا اعجاز صحیح رای کے لحاظ سے اس پر مبنی ہے کہ ادائے معنی کا طریقہ تمام طرق اداء سے ابلغ ہے اور اس کا اعجاز بسبب بلاغت کے ہے ۔ فباعتبار انه چونکہ اعجاز فی الکلام میں شرط ہے کہ وہ اپنے جمیع ما عدا طرق سے ابلغ ہو اس لئے وه طریق واحد ہو سکتا ہے جس میں تعدد نہیں ہوتا اس لئے عروۃ الاعجاز میں لفظ عروہ واحد اختیار کیا بخلاف سحر الکلام کے کہ وہ دقت کلام اور ماخذ کے لطیف ہونے کو کہتے ہیں لہذا اس کے طرق متعددہ اور مراتب مختلفہ ہو سکتے ہیں اس لئے اہداب السحر بلفظ الجمع کہا ہرب ثوب ان پھندنوں وغیرہ کو کہا جاتا ہے جو اطراف
His saying: 'Because i'jāz' — the author, while interpreting i'jāz, said that i'jāz is to express the meaning in a manner that is more eloquent and more effective than all other modes of expression. The commentator said regarding this: 'This is not the interpretation of the concept of i'jāz al-kalam', i.e., this is not the explanation of the concept of i'jāz of speech, because it is not necessary that i'jāz be with eloquence; rather, i'jāz in speech is that speech reaches a level where it cannot be challenged and producing its like becomes beyond human power. It is derived from 'a'jiztuhu' meaning 'made him incapable'. Now it is not necessary that the cause of i'jāz be eloquence; rather, various matters can be causes of i'jāz. This is why different reasons are given for the Qur'an being miraculous, despite agreement on its being miraculous. So it was said: 'it is due to its eloquence' — meaning the Qur'an is miraculous because it is at a high level of eloquence that human power cannot reach. And it was said: 'due to its predictive power' — i.e., some say the Qur'an is miraculous because it contains reports of the unseen, since humans do not have the power to report the unseen; this is completely outside their scientific limits. This reason was rejected because a sura that does not contain reports of the unseen would not be miraculous, but this is wrong; rather, every sura, indeed the length of a sura, is miraculous even if it does not contain reports of the unseen. And it was said: 'due to its unique style' — i.e., its style is strange and wondrous, different from both the prose and poetry styles of the eloquent and fluent, and those people were unfamiliar with this Qur'anic style. And it was said: 'due to the diversion of Allah' — meaning Allah, with His overwhelming power, turned the intellects of the challengers away from challenging it and bringing its like, and diverted them; they were not allowed to challenge it at all. This reason is also rejected because it would imply that a speech that is at the lowest level of eloquence, if only the intellects are diverted from challenging it, would have a stronger i'jāz, because despite being challengeable, it could not be challenged; this is more obvious in 'sarf al-'uqul' and should be stronger in i'jāz. But the intended meaning: the author's intent is to present the correct view regarding the i'jāz of the Qur'an, i.e., the i'jāz of the Qur'an according to the correct view is based on the fact that the way of expressing meaning is more eloquent than all other modes, and its i'jāz is due to eloquence. So because it is a condition in i'jāz of speech that it be more eloquent than all other modes, therefore it can be a single way with no multiplicity; hence in 'urwat al-i'jāz' the singular 'urwa' was chosen, unlike sihr al-kalām, which is called subtleness of speech and delicacy of source; therefore its ways can be multiple and its degrees different; hence 'ahdāb al-sihr' was said in the plural; 'harb' means things like hem-strings, etc., which are at the edges.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the concept of i'jāz (inimitability) of the Qur'an. The commentator first corrects the author's definition: i'jāz is not merely the most eloquent expression but the quality of being unchallengeable. He then presents four opinions on the cause of the Qur'an's miracle: (1) its eloquence, (2) its reports of the unseen, (3) its unique style, and (4) divine diversion of challengers. He refutes the second and fourth arguments. The second is refuted because even suras without ghayb reports are miraculous. The fourth leads to absurd consequences: if only diversion makes it miraculous, then any lowly speech would be more miraculous if challengers are diverted. The commentator then clarifies the author's intended meaning: the correct view is that i'jāz is based on eloquence (balāghah). Finally, he explains why the word 'urwah (singular) is used for i'jāz: i'jāz is a single path because it must surpass all others, while sihr (magic/sorcery) can have multiple ways, hence the plural 'ahdāb.

Key Terms
اعجاز (i'jāz): The quality of a speech that makes it impossible to challenge or produce its like, beyond human capability.
بلاغت (balāghah): Eloquence; the high standard of expression that cannot be matched.
صرف (sarf): Divine diversion; the theory that Allah prevented challengers from producing similar speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct definition of i'jāz in speech according to the commentator?
A) The most eloquent expression of meaning
B) Speech that cannot be challenged and whose like cannot be produced
C) Speech containing reports of the unseen
D) Speech with a unique style

Key Takeaway
I'jāz of the Qur'an is defined as its quality of being beyond human imitation, and the correct view attributes this to its unparalleled eloquence (balāghah), not to incidental factors like predictions or divine prevention.

The Definition of I'jaz
Definition and terminological analysis of i'jaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of i'jaz as 'speech rendering others incapable' and contrast it with sihr.

Core Idea
I'jaz means rendering others incapable of producing similar speech, regardless of whether eloquence is the cause.

This lesson examines the foundational definition of i'jaz (inimitability) as presented in the commentary. It clarifies that i'jaz fundamentally means rendering opponents incapable of producing a similar text, and that this does not necessarily require eloquence, though eloquence is the accepted cause in the Hanafi school. You will also learn the distinction between i'jaz and sihr in terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of i'jaz
Locate the sentence defining i'jaz in the passage. The definition is: 'speech reaching a level where it cannot be contested and its like is beyond human capacity.'
This is derived from the root 'a-j-z' meaning to render incapable.
2
Note that eloquence is not necessary
The commentator stresses that eloquence is not a necessary condition for i'jaz; other causes could exist. This shows that i'jaz is broader than just eloquence.
This is important because some scholars wrongly equate i'jaz only with eloquence.
3
Contrast i'jaz with sihr
Sihr is defined as subtlety of speech and fineness of origin, and it can have multiple methods and levels (hence the plural 'ahdab). I'jaz is singular (hence 'urwa).
The metaphor: 'urwa (a single strong handle) for i'jaz; 'ahdab (multiple fringes) for sihr.
4
Understand the significance of singular vs. plural
The use of 'urwat al-i'jaz (singular) emphasizes that i'jaz is a single, strong method that renders all others inferior. Sihr, by contrast, can vary in quality.
This linguistic choice highlights the uniqueness and invulnerability of the Quran's i'jaz.
