Clarifying, he said: The definition of the Quran is that which has been transmitted to us in its entirety. The term 'book' and 'Quran' is used by the usuliyyun (jurisprudents), unlike the theologians for whom 'Quran' applies to the eternal attribute that is uncreated. For the usuliyyun, it applies both to the whole as a whole and to every part that can serve as evidence for a ruling, because they discuss the Book of Allah as evidence for rulings, and each verse serves as evidence, not only the whole. So they needed to seek common attributes between the whole and the parts to use in a definition that applies to both whole and part. For example, being miraculous, being revealed to the Messenger, being written in the mushafs, being transmitted via tawatur—these are attributes common equally to whole and part. Some scholars considered all these attributes in the definition; some only considered revelation and inimitability because these are necessary attributes in every era of the Quran, unlike writing and transmission, as in the time of the Messenger the Quran existed without being written in mushafs or transmitted to us. So these are not necessary and should not be used in the definition. Others considered revelation, writing, and transmission because the purpose of the definition is for those who did not witness revelation or the time of the Messenger, so they know the Quran through transmission and writing in mushafs. In their time, these are inseparable, so they are clearer in indicating the intended meaning. Thus defining with these is beneficial for them, unlike inimitability because it is not clear for them, as knowledge of inimitability is the work of scholars specialized in ma'ani and bayan. And it does not include every part because inimitability is only of a surah or its equivalent, as Allah's saying 'fa'tu bi suratin min mithlihi' indicates. The author sufficed with mentioning 'transmitted in the mushafs' because with it the purpose of definition is achieved and exclusion of everything other than the Quran is obtained, because all other heavenly books, divine hadiths, abrogated recitation verses, and prophetic hadiths are not transmitted between the covers of the mushafs. Because it is a name for this well-known specific collection known to people, even children know it, and all mentioned are not transmitted in this well-known mushaf. And the irregular recitations (qira'at shadhdhah) are excluded by the condition of tawatur because their transmission, even if in some specific mushafs, is via ahad (isolated) or mashhur (well-known), like those specific to Ubayy ibn Ka'b's mushaf or Ibn Mas'ud's, but in any case not by tawatur, so they are excluded. There is no need to mention 'inzal' and 'i'jaz', nor the word 'bila shubhah' for emphasis of tawatur, because without them the intended exclusion is achieved. As for basmalah: the famous madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, mentioned in most early books, is that the basmalah is not part of the Quran except for the basmalah in Surah al-Naml which is part of a verse. That is part of the Quran. The basmalah at the beginnings of surahs is not part of the Quran. However, according to the later scholars, the correct view is that according to the Imam, the basmalah at the beginnings of surahs is only one verse of the Quran, revealed to separate between surahs. The evidence is that the basmalah is written in the Quran in the script of the Quran, and no rejection or disapproval from the Salaf is transmitted, even though the rule of purifying the Quran from non-Quran is emphasized, so it is known that the basmalah is part of the Quran. Several issues used to create doubt about its Quranicity. One: if one suffices with the basmalah in prayer, the prayer is not valid, even though according to the Imam one verse is sufficient for the obligatory recitation. The answer: it is due to doubt - it is not a complete verse, so the obligatory amount is not fulfilled. Second issue: for the junub and menstruating woman...

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Definition of Quran: Identification, Not Essence
Clarifying definition type and responding to homonymy objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition for identification (ta'rif bi al-tashkhis) and definition for essence (ta'rif bi al-mahiyya) in Hanafi usul, and apply this distinction to clarify the status of the Quran as transmitted speech.

Core Idea
The Quran's definition is to pinpoint which meaning is intended, not to define its eternal essence.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, definitions serve different purposes. This passage addresses a subtle objection: if 'Quran' applies both to the whole and to parts, does its definition commit the error of generalizing a homonym? The commentator clarifies that the definition 'what is transmitted to us' is merely for identification, picking out the recited speech as opposed to the eternal attribute. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly handling homonyms in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The opponent claims that if 'Quran' is literally true for both the whole and the part, then using it to define a universal term would imply generality in a homonym (umum al-mushtarak), which is invalid.
This objection hinges on the assumption that 'Quran' has only one coinage.
2
Understand the Response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between two coinages: one specific for the whole, and one general that covers both whole and part. The general coinage avoids the problem of umum al-mushtarak because a single intended meaning is used.
The response also notes that the definition is for identification, not essence.
3
Locate the Clarification on Definition Type
The commentator explicitly states that the definition is not for essence (mahiyya) but for identification (tashkhis). This resolves the confusion about whether the definition must capture all essential attributes.
The phrase 'ليس هذا تعريف ماهية الكتاب بل تشخيصه' is key.
4
Apply the Distinction
Recognize that 'Quran' is a homonym (mushtarak) between eternal speech and recited speech. The definition 'ma nukkila ilayna' singles out the recited speech via the condition of transmission, which is an identifying feature.
This prevents confusion when encountering the term in legal texts.

فإن قيل فالكتاب بالمعنى الثاني إلى بالمعنى الصادق على الكل والبعض بل يصح تفسيره بالقرآن جب[؟] كأن القرآن توكل من حيث الكل يقال ألا هذا كتاب بالمعنى الكلي تفسير يمكن. قلنا نعم لأنه إطلاق القرآن على البعض حقيقي كما في الكل حقيقة لذا هذا تفسير الكتاب يمكن. فان قيل فلزم عموم المشترك لأنه إذا للبعض حقيقة وللكل إطلاق على سبيل الحقيقة فهو موضوع للمعنيين فهذا شرك بين المعنيين وفي إطلاق واحد مراد جميع معاني المشترك عموم في المشترك وهو ممنوع لأنه لا عموم في المشترك. قلنا ليس معنى كونه حقيقة اتخذ هذا سؤال جواب أي أنه في بعض حقيقة كالكل ليس معناه أنه وضع للبعض خاصة كما وضع للكل خاصة فيلزم من حمل على الكل والبعض عموم المشترك بل مرادنا أن له وضعا خاصا للكل فقط ووضعا عاما يشمل الكل والبعض أعني الكلام المنقول في المصحف تواترا فبذلك بوضع واحد يكون حقيقة في الكل والبعض. ومرادنا هذا المعنى العام وبهذا التفسير الكتاب بالمعنى الكلي يمكن. ولا يكون من عموم المشترك في شئ لأن المشترك معنى واحد مراد. قال المصنف في التوضيح ترددت تحقيقاتي هذا الموضع الآن أن هذا التعريف للقرآن أي أنواع التعريف هو. فإن تمام الجواب موقوف على هذا التحقيق. وليس هذا تعريف ماهية الكتاب بل تشخيصه. ولا القرآن بل تشخيصه. فالتعريف ما نقل إلينا سواء كان تعريف كتاب أو قرآن فهو لتعيين وتشخيص الكتاب المقصود. لان القران يطلق على الكلام الأزلي القديم وعلى الكلام المقروء بالألسنة. فالتعريف ما نقل إلينا يخصص المحتمل الأول. فكأنه قيل: أي المعنيين تريد؟ فقال: ما نقل إلينا.
If it is said: The Book (al-Kitab) in the second sense applies to the whole and the part, but it is permissible to interpret it by the Quran, but the Quran is said of the whole only. So can this be an interpretation of the Book in the universal sense? We say: Yes, because the application of Quran to a part is real (haqiqi) just as it is real for the whole; therefore, it can be an interpretation of the Book. If it is said: This necessitates general application of a homonym (umum al-mushtarak), because if it is real for the part and also applied to the whole in a real sense, then it is a shared term between two meanings, and intending all meanings of a homonym in a single usage is generality in the homonym (umum al-mushtarak), which is prohibited because there is no generality in a homonym. We say: The meaning of it being real for the part is not that it is specifically coined for the part as it is specifically coined for the whole, which would necessitate generality. Rather, our meaning is that it has a specific coinage for the whole and a general coinage that includes both the whole and the part, meaning the transmitted speech in the Mushaf via tawatur. So with this single coinage, it is real for both the whole and the part. And this general meaning is what we intend, and with this interpretation it can be an interpretation of the universal Book. And this does not fall under generality of a homonym at all, because the homonym has only one meaning intended. The author said in al-Tawdih: My investigations have hesitated at this point that this definition of the Quran—what type of definition is it? The completion of the answer depends on this investigation. This is not a definition of the essence of the Book but of its identification. Similarly, it is not of the essence of the Quran but of its identification. So the definition 'what is transmitted to us'—whether it is a definition of the Book or the Quran—serves to specify and identify the intended Book. Because the term 'Quran' is applied to the eternal speech and to the recited speech. The definition 'what is transmitted to us' specifies the first possibility. It is as if he was asked: Which of the two meanings do you intend? He answered: What is transmitted to us.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary discusses the proper definition of the Quran. The author responds to an objection: if the word 'Quran' applies both to the whole and the part, how can it define a universal meaning (al-kitab)? The answer is that 'Quran' is used literally for both; it has a dual coinage—one specific for the whole and one general for both. This avoids the problem of 'umum al-mushtarak' (generalizing a homonym), which is invalid. The commentator then clarifies that the definition (ما نقل إلينا) is not intended to describe the essence (mahiyya) of the Quran but to specify and identify the intended referent—the recited speech transmitted to us—as opposed to the eternal attribute of speech. This is crucial because the term 'Quran' is a homonym (mushtarak) between the eternal speech (kalam azali) and the recited words. The definition picks out the latter, which is what is transmitted.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): لفظ وضع لمعنيين أو أكثر وضعاً واحداً لكل منهما على سبيل الحقيقة
تعريف تشخيص (ta'rif bi al-tashkhis): تعريف يهدف إلى تعيين المسمى لا بيان حقيقته
تعريف ماهية (ta'rif bi al-mahiyya): تعريف يبين حقيقة الشيء وذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To describe the essence of the Quran
B) To specify the recited speech as opposed to the eternal attribute
C) To prove the Quran is uncreated

Key Takeaway
The definition of Quran serves to identify the transmitted recited speech, not to define its eternal essence.

Al-Quran as Source

Why the Quran Cannot Be Defined: The Concept of Shakhsi
Usul definition with logical critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two interpretations of why the Quran is considered a personal entity (shakhsi) in Hanafi usul and evaluate Ibn al-Hajib's definition for circularity.

Core Idea
The Quran is a unique personal entity; it cannot be defined by logical definition but only identified by pointing.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in usul al-fiqh: can the Quran be defined? The Hanafi response is nuanced, involving the concept of shakhsi (personal entity). The author provides two interpretations and then critiques Ibn al-Hajib's alternative definition, highlighting the risk of circularity (dawr). By understanding this, you will grasp why the Quran's unique nature affects how it is used as a source of law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of defining a unique entity
Recognize that a truly unique entity cannot be captured by a genus-differentia definition. The Quran's exact wording is singular, so any definition would either be incomplete or circular.
This is a logical (aqli) argument.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'shakhsi'
The text gives two ta'wils: (1) the Quran is truly personal because it is the exact words of Jibril; (2) even if not truly personal, it is considered personal in our terminology because it is a specific composition that only changes by change of locus (e.g., reciters). Both lead to indefinability.
The second interpretation is a terminological concession.
3
Examine Ibn al-Hajib's definition
Ibn al-Hajib defined the Quran as 'speech sent down for miraculousness by a surah from it' to avoid circularity. Understand this definition and its intent.
This is an alternative approach from outside the Hanafi school.
4
Evaluate his definition for circularity
If the definition aims to define the essence of Quran, it requires knowing the essence of 'surah', which itself is defined as part of Quran, causing circularity. If it only aims to identify, it is redundant. Therefore, it fails to solve the problem.
This critique shows the strength of the Hanafi approach.

Knowledge is obtained and by that knowledge. With it, He says that the Noble Quran is a personal matter. So know that when Jibril brought down the Quran, the exact words that were established with the tongue of Jibril. If the name 'Quran' is only that exact composition that was established with the tongue of Jibril and recited, then the Quran is completely a personal entity that cannot accept multiplicity at all. Rather it is only established with the tongue of Jibril, so then the Quran is truly personal, therefore it cannot be subject to definition. But if 'Quran' is not only the name of that personal composition, but rather the specific words that are composed with a specific composition, whether Jibril recites them or Zayd and Shimr recite them, then it cannot be truly personal because it is then subject to multiplicity. So then what is the meaning of 'upon that the personal is not defined'? So he said: our saying 'upon that the personal is not defined' has two interpretations. One interpretation is that by 'personal' we do not mean it is truly personal, rather we mean that the Quran is the name of a speech composed with a specific composition, so such a specific thing is also not subject to definition. So the phrase 'upon that the personal is not defined' is the proof that the Quran is not subject to definition. The second interpretation is that we say: by 'personal' we mean that the Quran is the name of those specific words with which those specific attributes are considered that enter into that specific composition. Even if it is not truly personal, in our terminology it is personal, and there is no dispute in terminology. Because accidents reach such a limit of determination due to their specific attributes that in themselves they do not accept multiplicity and difference, only by virtue of their locus. Like a specific poem that does not accept multiplicity except by multiplicity of locus. So we call such a poem personal, and personal in this sense also does not accept definition. So our intention by the Quran being personal is this. So it is that if one asks about the Quran, the answer cannot be without reciting from beginning to end and saying that the Quran is called those specific words recited. So this is what we call personal, which is not subject to definition but only its determination can be by pointing. And Ibn al-Hajib defined the Quran to avoid this circularity: 'It is the speech sent down for miraculousness by a surah from it.' So he did not use the word. But if Ibn al-Hajib intends by it to define the essence of the Quran, then he does not avoid circularity, because then knowing the essence of surah becomes necessary. So if one asks 'what is surah?' inevitably one must say 'a part of the Quran', so circularity occurs: the essence of Quran depends on the essence of surah and vice versa. But if Ibn al-Hajib does not intend to define the essence but rather identification and specification, and by 'surah' he means the conventional part among people, then the issue of circularity does not arise. So Ibn al-Hajib's deviation from the famous definition to this unfamiliar one is without benefit.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of the Quran and whether it can be defined as a 'personal entity' (شخصی). The author first explains two interpretations of why the Quran is called 'personal' and therefore indefinable. The first interpretation: the Quran is truly personal because it is the exact words delivered by Jibril, so it cannot be defined like any unique entity. The second interpretation: even if it is not truly personal, it is considered personal in Hanafi terminology because it is a specific composition that only changes by changing its locus (e.g., reciters). Then the text presents Ibn al-Hajib's definition to avoid circularity: 'الكلام المنزل للاعجاز بسورة منه' (Speech sent down for miraculousness by a surah from it). The author critiques this: if it aims to define the essence, it falls into circularity because 'surah' itself is defined as a part of the Quran. If it is only for identification, then it is unnecessary. The discussion is rational (dalil aqli) and focuses on the logical problems of defining a unique entity.

Key Terms
شخصی (shakhṣī): An entity that is unique and cannot be multiplied by its essence; in the context of Quran, it means the exact words as revealed, which cannot have multiple instances without change of locus.
حد (ḥadd): A logical definition that specifies the essence of a thing by genus and differentia.
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition, where the definiendum is used to define the definiens, causing a logical fallacy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the second interpretation of 'shakhsi' still make the Quran indefinable?
A) Because it is truly personal like a unique entity.
B) Because even if not truly personal, it is personal in terminology and only changes by change of locus, so it cannot be defined by genus and differentia.
C) Because it can be defined by its miraculous nature.
D) Because it is not personal at all and can be defined.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is a personal entity (shakhsi) that cannot be defined by logical definition; it is only identifiable by pointing to its specific revealed words.

The Unity of the Qur'an: A Rational Proof
Establishing that the Qur'an remains one despite multiple reciters via a rational proof and extending the principle to other authored works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the identity of a revealed text is based on its composition (mu'allaf makhsus) and not on its locus, using the rational argument (dalil aqli) from this passage.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is one specific composition regardless of who recites it; its identity does not depend on the reciter's tongue.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi usul principle: the Qur'an is defined by its words and order, not by the accident of its occurrence in a particular reciter or angel. Using a rational argument (dalil aqli), the author proves that if the Qur'an's identity were tied to its locus (e.g., the tongue of Jibril), then multiple reciters would create multiple Qur'ans, contradicting our certainty that we all recite the same revelation. This principle is then extended to any book, poem, or science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Locate the author's main claim about what defines the Qur'an. Look for the term 'مولف مخصوص' (specific composition).
The thesis is that the Qur'an is a mu'allaf makhsus, independent of the reciter.
2
Understand the hypothetical
What would follow if the Qur'an's identity depended on its locus (being on Jibril's tongue)? The author presents a counterfactual.
The hypothetical leads to multiple Qur'ans, which is false.
3
Trace the rational argument
The author uses the principle that accidents (a'rad) are specified by their locus. If the locus changes, the accident changes. Apply this to the Qur'an.
If the Qur'an were an accident inhering in Jibril's tongue, then different reciters would be different loci, making the Qur'an multiple.
4
Observe the expansion
See how the author expands the same reasoning from the Qur'an to any book, poem, or science. Note the phrase 'وكذا الكلام في كل كتاب او شعر'.
The principle generalizes: any authored work is defined by its composition, not by its reader.
5
Derive the usul principle
Extract the general rule: the identity of a composition is determined solely by its text and order, independent of any accidental locus.
This is a key usul principle for defining the Qur'an and other texts.

Source Text

قوله على ان الحق هذا یعنی حق یہی ہے کہ قران مجید اسی مولف مخصوص کا نام ہے جو متلفظین اور پڑھنے والوں کے مختلف اور متعدد ہونے سے مختلف و متعدد نہیں ہوتا۔ للقطع یعنی یہ یقینی اور قطعی ہے کہ اس مولف مخصوص کو ہم میں سے جو شخص بھی پڑھے یہ وہی قرآن مجید ہے جو رسول صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم پر بلسان جبریل نازل ہوا تھا۔ ولو كان عباس ، اگر قران مجید صرف وہی جزی مخصوص کا نام ہوتا ملسان جبریل علیہ السلام قائم تھا تو پھر یہ قرآن مجید جو ہم پڑھتے ہیں بعینہ وہی نہیں ہوگا بلکہ اس کا مائل اور مشابہ ہوگا ضرورة ان الاعراض الخ یعنی اعراض کا پورا تعیین اور تشخص اس وقت ہوتا ہے جب اس کا محل متشخص و متعین ہو اور تعدد محال کے ساتھ اعراض متعدد ہو جاتے ہیں اب قران مجید کے تشخص میں تشخص محل یعنی قیام بلسان جبریل بھی شامل کر دیا جائے تو جو اور اکسنہ ادا کرتی ہیں تو یہ تعدد محل ہوا پھر تو قران مجید متعدد اور مختلف ہو جائیں گے حالانکہ ایسے نہیں بلکہ ہم سب وہی قرآن مجید تلاوت کرتے ہیں جو رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم پر بواسطہ جبریل ۱۴ نازل ہوا تھا تو معلوم ہو کہ تشخص محل کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ وكذا الكلام في كل كتاب او شعر الو یعنی اسی طرح کلام ہوگی ہر کتاب و شعر کے بارے میں جو کسی ایک شخص کی طرف منسوب ہے کہ وہ بھی اسی مولف مخصوص کا نام ہے خواہ اس کے پڑھنے والا زید ہو یا عمر و بکر وغیرہ واذا تحقق هذا فالعلوم جب یہ متحقق ہوا کہ کتاب و شعر کے اندر کلمات مخصوصه با ترکیب و ترتیب مخصوصہ کا اعتبار ہوتا ہے اور تعیین محل کا اعتبار نہیں ہوتا تو جو علوم مخصوصہ ہیں وہ بھی اسی طرح کے خصوصیتہ کے حامل ہیں مثلا علم النحو بھی قواعد اور قوانین مخصوصہ کا نام ہو گا خواہ اس کا عالم اور جاننے والا زید ہو یا عمر الحاصل یہ کہ ان تمام امور میں وحدۃ محمل کے علاوہ باقی تمام امور میں وحدة معبتر ہے بہر حال ان اشیا کو تعدد محل کی وجہ سے اشیاء مختلفہ قرار نہیں دیا جاتا بلکہ جس میں محل کے ساتھ قائم ہو کہا جاتا کہ یہ وہی مخصوص شعر ہے اور مخصوص
His statement: 'But the truth is this' means the truth is that the Noble Qur'an is the name of this specific composition, which does not become different or multiple due to the difference and multiplicity of reciters and readers. It is decisive and certain that whoever among us reads this specific composition, it is the very same Noble Qur'an that was revealed to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, through the tongue of Jibril. And if it were otherwise, if the Qur'an were only the name of that specific part which existed through the tongue of Jibril, peace be upon him, then this Qur'an that we recite would not be exactly the same, but rather similar and resembling it. Necessarily, the full determination and specification of accidents occurs when their locus is specified and determined, and with the multiplicity of loci, accidents become multiple. Now, if in the specification of the Qur'an, the specification of the locus, i.e., its occurrence through the tongue of Jibril, is also included, then the reciters perform it, and this is multiplicity of loci; then the Qur'an would become multiple and different, while it is not so. Rather, we all recite the same Qur'an that was revealed to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, through Jibril. So it is known that the specification of the locus is not considered. Similarly, the speech applies to every book or poem that is attributed to a single person: that it is also the name of that specific composition, whether its reciter is Zayd or 'Amr or Bakr, etc. And when this is established, then the sciences: when it is established that in a book and a poem, the specific words with specific composition and order are considered, and the determination of the locus is not considered, then the specific sciences also have the same specificity. For example, the science of grammar is the name of specific rules and principles, whether its knower and scholar is Zayd or 'Amr. The conclusion is that in all these matters, unity of the locus aside, unity is considered in all other matters. In any case, these things are not considered different things due to multiplicity of loci; rather, it is said that this is the same specific poem and specific.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil aqli) establishing the unity of the Qur'an despite multiple reciters. The author argues that the Qur'an is defined as the specific composition (مولف مخصوص) — its words and order — not by the accident of its occurrence in a particular locus (e.g., the tongue of Jibril). If the latter were part of its definition, then each recitation would be a different Qur'an because the locus differs. But we all recite the same Qur'an, so the definition excludes the locus. This principle is extended to any book or poem attributed to an author: it remains the same regardless of who recites it. Finally, it is applied to sciences: for example, the science of grammar is defined by its rules, not by who knows it. This aligns with Hanafi usul that definitions focus on the essence (ذات) and not on accidental attributes like the mode of existence or the individual knower.

Key Terms
مولف مخصوص (mu'allaf makhsus): A specific composition defined by its words and order, not by its locus or individual reciter
تشخص محل (tashakhkhs al-mahall): The specification of the locus or substrate where an accident inheres

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the identity of the Qur'an does not depend on its locus (e.g., the tongue of Jibril)?
A) Because the locus is unknown.
B) Because that would make the Qur'an multiple when recited by different people.
C) Because the Qur'an is a universal, not a particular.
D) Because the Qur'an is only in the Preserved Tablet.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is defined as a specific composition (mu'allaf makhsus) of words and order; its identity does not depend on the locus of its recitation, ensuring unity across all reciters.

Al-Quran as Source

The Quran as a Personal Entity: Definition and Distinction
Explaining why the Quran is a personal entity and how it can be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quran is a personal entity (shakhsi) in Hanafi usul and apply the distinction between definition of essence (tahdid) and definition of distinction (tamyiz).

Core Idea
The Quran is a personal entity that cannot be defined by essence, but only by indication or by distinguishing description.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Tawdih addresses a key Hanafi position: the Quran is not a universal concept but a particular, personal entity (shakhsi). This means its true reality cannot be captured by a conventional definition (hadd). Instead, we can only point to it – by reciting it from beginning to end or by a description that distinguishes it from everything else. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind this view and explore the two interpretations of 'shakhsi' offered by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by asserting that the Quran is a personal entity (shakhsi). Note the analogy with a person like Zaid, who can be identified by a description but not defined in essence. The Quran similarly cannot be defined by a standard definition because it is a particular, not a universal.
The claim is that the Quran is shakhsi in the sense that it is a specific text, not a generic concept.
2
Understand the argument from locus
The author argues that if the Quran were tied to a specific locus (e.g., Jibril's tongue), it would become multiple when recited by different people. But the Quran remains one and the same regardless of reciter, so its identity does not depend on any locus. This proves it is not a mere accident (arad) that changes with locus.
This is a direct response to those who might think the Quran is only the specific part that stood on Jibril's tongue.
3
Distinguish the two interpretations of 'shakhsi'
The commentator gives two interpretations of why the Quran is called shakhsi. First, by comparison (tashbih): just as a real personal entity cannot be defined, the Quran's true knowledge requires recitation or indication. Second, in terminology (istilah): we call it personal because its multiplicity cannot occur without multiplicity of locus, so it is treated as a single personal entity.
Both interpretations lead to the same conclusion: the Quran cannot be defined by a hadd that captures its essence.
4
Apply the distinction: tamyiz vs. tahdid
The passage concludes that if true knowledge of essence is not required, we can define the Quran by distinction (tamyiz). For example: 'The Quran is that collection transmitted mutawatiran between the covers of the mus'haf.' This definition does not give the essence but distinguishes it from all other books.
This is a practical definition used in usul and fiqh.

جار اللہ ہ محشری نے تفسیر قرآن کے بارہ میں تصنیف فرمایا ہے یا کہا جاتا ہے کہ نحو ایک علم ہے جس کے اندر کلم کے احوال باعتبار اعراب و بنا کی بحث کی جاتی ہے تو اس قسم کی تعریف کے ساتھ تمیز حاصل ہو سکتی ہے۔ قوله فان الاعراض تتھی یعنی اعراض اپنے مشخصات کی وجہ سے ایسے مرتبہ تعیین
It is possible, for example, in answer to 'Who is Zaid?' that one says: 'The one who came to you today alone before everyone,' meaning Zaid is the person who came to you today first alone. So in terms of existence before external [reality], there cannot be two such persons, but rationally despite this identification, multiplicity is possible in it, and it is not impossible rationally for it to apply to many. This kind of determination and identification can only be obtained with indication. His statement: 'Rather, the truth is this' means that the truth is that the Noble Quran is the name of that specific composed text which does not become different or multiple due to the difference and multiplicity of speakers and readers. 'For certainty' means that it is certain and decisive that whichever of us reads this specific composed text, it is the same Quran that was revealed to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) by the tongue of Jibril. 'And if it were [only the specific part that stood on Jibril's tongue]...' If the Quran were the name of only that specific part that stood on the tongue of Jibril (peace be upon him), then this Quran that we read would not be exactly that, but rather similar and analogous to it. 'Necessarily, accidents...' That is, the full determination and specification of accidents occurs when their locus is specified and determined, and with the multiplicity of loci the accidents become multiple. Now if in the specification of the Quran, the specification of the locus, i.e., its standing on Jibril's tongue, were also included, then whoever recites it would have a multiplicity of loci, and then the Quran would become multiple and different, whereas it is not so; rather we all recite the same Quran that was revealed to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) via Jibril. So it is known that the specification of the locus is not considered. 'And similarly the discussion regarding every book or poem...' That is, similarly the discussion applies to every book or poem attributed to one person, that it is also the name of that specific authorial text, whether its reader is Zaid or 'Amr or Bakr, etc. 'And when this is established, then the sciences...' When it is established that in book and poem, the consideration is of specific words with specific composition and order, and the consideration of locus is not, then specific sciences also have similar specificity. For example, grammar is also the name of specific rules and laws, whether its scholar and knower is Zaid or 'Amr. The conclusion is that in all these matters, unity of referent is considered besides all other matters. In any case, these things are not considered different things due to multiplicity of locus; rather, in whatever locus it stands, it is said that this is the same specific poem and the same specific science and book; it is not said that these things change with change of locus. 'So on this basis...' Now, according to this estimation and true verification, the Noble Quran is not the name of that real personal entity which stood with the revered Jibril (peace be upon him), but rather it is the name of whatever locus it stands upon. So what meaning would the author's statement 'that it is personal...' have, when the Quran is not a real personal entity? So he said: 'It has two interpretations.' One of them: that the real personal entity... its summary is that the Quran is carried upon comparison, i.e., just as a real personal entity is not capable of definition because the true knowledge of a personal entity cannot be obtained without indication or some other similar determinant and identifier, similarly the true knowledge of the Quran cannot be obtained without it, such that one recites from beginning to end and says that the Quran is the name of these specific words in this specific order, and it cannot be defined because definitions cannot specify a personal entity in this way. 'And the second...' The second interpretation of its being personal is that one authorial text with specific composition, whose multiplicity cannot occur without multiplicity of locus, such that we in our terminology consider it personal. That is, even if it is not a real specific personal entity, it is terminologically personal, and it is binding that it does not accept definition, because the true knowledge of such a personal matter cannot be obtained without indication; one recites from beginning to end and says that the Quran is the name of this specific recited text. 'It is not hidden that the discussion...' That is a preamble to the previous: that when the Quran is a personal matter, its true knowledge cannot be obtained with definition; rather recitation and indication are necessary. Yes, if true knowledge is not intended from definition, rather only distinction from other things is intended, then that distinction is possible with definition, i.e., in its definition one says: 'The Quran is the name of that collection which is transmitted between the two covers of the mus'haf with tawatur.' Then with this definition it becomes distinguished from other things. Just as it is said: 'Al-Kashshaf is a book authored by Jarallah al-Zamakhshari on the interpretation of the Quran,' or it is said: 'Nahw is a science in which the states of words regarding inflection and construction are discussed.' With this kind of definition, distinction can be obtained. His statement: 'For accidents reach...' means that accidents, due to their specifics, reach such a level of determination.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh discussion on the nature of the Quran and its definition. The author is explaining a point from Al-Tawdih: that the Quran is not a physical entity tied to a specific locus (like Jibril's tongue) but rather an attribute of Allah, consisting of specific words in a specific order. He argues that the Quran remains one and the same regardless of who recites it, because its identity is based on the composed text, not on the accident of its recitation by different persons. The passage addresses the concern that if the Quran were defined by its locus, it would become multiple, but it is not so. The author then extends the analogy to books and poems, and even sciences like grammar. The core lesson is about 'tahdid' (definition) of a 'shakhsi' (personal) entity: a real personal entity cannot be defined except by demonstration, but terminologically, we can define the Quran by distinguishing it from others, e.g., as the transmitted collection in the mus'haf. This is a Hanafi perspective that upholds the uncreated nature of the Quran as an attribute of Allah.

Key Terms
شخصی (shakhsi): A personal entity that cannot be known by definition but only by indication or recitation.
تحدید (tahdid): Definition that captures the essence (haqiqah) of something; here the Quran's essence cannot be defined, but it can be distinguished.
اعراض (a'rad): Accidents that inhere in a locus; their identity depends on the locus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why cannot the Quran be defined by a standard definition (hadd) like ordinary objects?
A) Because it is a physical book that varies in every copy.
B) Because it is a personal entity (shakhsi) that can only be known by indication or recitation.
C) Because it is created and multiple in essence.
D) Because it is an accident that depends on a locus.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is one unchangeable text; its identity does not depend on any created locus, and it can only be defined by distinguishing it from other books, not by capturing its essence.

The Quran as a Personal Entity: Hanafi Definition and Two Interpretations
Analyzing the definition of Quran as personal entity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quran is considered a personal entity (shakhsi) in Hanafi usul and distinguish the two interpretations (metaphorical and terminological).

Core Idea
The Quran is a personal entity whose true reality can only be known by recitation, not by a definition limited by boundaries.

In Hanafi usul, defining the Quran is not a trivial matter. Scholars debated whether the Quran is a 'personal entity' (shakhsi) that cannot be captured by a standard definition (hadd). This lesson unpacks the two interpretations presented in the text: a metaphorical comparison and a terminological usage. You will learn why the Quran resists definition and how it can still be distinguished from other speech through a descriptive definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of a personal entity (shakhsi)
Recognize that a personal entity is unique, not subject to multiplicity, and its reality is known only by direct indication or recitation, not by a definition.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between real personal (haqiqi) and terminological personal (istilahi).
2
Identify the two interpretations for the Quran being shakhsi
The text gives two ta'wils: (1) metaphorical – the Quran's indefinability is likened to a real personal entity; (2) terminological – any composition that cannot be multiplied without change of locus is called personal.
Both interpretations reject the possibility of a true definition (tahdid) for the Quran.
3
Distinguish between defining for reality vs. mere distinction
If the goal is knowledge of the true reality (haqiqah), definition fails. But if the goal is only distinction from other things (tamyiz), a descriptive definition is possible, e.g., 'the collection transmitted between the two covers by tawatur'.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction that a personal entity cannot be defined yet scholars do define it operationally.
4
Analyze Ibn al-Hajib's definition and avoid circularity
Ibn al-Hajib's definition is interpreted as a collective personal (majmu' shakhsi) due to the partitive 'min'. The commentator shows no circularity (dawr) arises because understanding 'surah' does not depend on understanding the Quran as a whole.
This careful analysis preserves logical consistency in definition.

Knowledge and book are change of place; with these matters, change is not said. Thus, the personal estimation of truth. Now according to this estimation and verification of truth, the Noble Quran is not the name of that personal real (entity) which was established with Hadrat Jibril (peace be upon him), but rather whatever it is established with, that is called the Noble Quran. So what meaning will be given to what the author said 'that it is personal' when the Quran is not personal real? He said: it has two interpretations: one is that the personal real... its summary is that it is carried on metaphor, meaning just as a personal real is not subject to definition, because knowledge of the reality of a personal (entity) cannot be obtained except by indication or something similar that determines and identifies it, similarly the knowledge of the reality of the Quran cannot be obtained without that, such that one recites from beginning to end and says that the Quran is the name of these specific words in a specific order, and it cannot be defined because definitions cannot determine personality in this way. The second is: this is the second interpretation of being personal. Summary: a specific composition, whose multiplicity cannot occur without multiplicity of locus, we in our terminology consider it personal. That is, even if it is not real personal, it is terminologically personal and strong because it does not accept change. A terminological personal is also not subject to definition because knowledge of the reality of this kind of personal matter cannot be obtained without indication, by reciting from beginning to end and saying that the Quran is the name of this specific recited (text). It is not hidden that the preceding statement is that when the Quran is a personal matter, its knowledge of reality cannot be by definition but rather recitation and indication are necessary. But if the intended from definition is not knowledge of reality but only distinction from other things, then this distinction is possible by definition, such as saying: define it by saying that Quran is the name of that collection which is transmitted between the two covers by tawatur. Thus by this definition it becomes distinct from others, as it is said that Kashshaf is a book authored by Jarallah Zamakhshari on the interpretation of Quran, or it is said that Nahw is a science in which the conditions of words regarding inflection and construction are discussed. By this kind of definition distinction can be obtained. His saying: 'then the accidents' meaning accidents due to their specifics reach such a level of determination that multiplicity in them is removed, they apply to only one thing; however, multiplicity can occur due to multiplicity of locus, like the saying of Imru' al-Qais for example: 'Decree from the one who reminded me of a beloved and a dwelling' to the end of the poem. Now this saying by its specifics, i.e., specific composition among specific letters, words, and specific verses, then their specific movements and pauses which are obtained from it, due to these it has reached a level that multiplicity and applying to many is not possible. But multiplicity due to the utterance is possible, meaning if Zayd pronounces it and 'Amr and others, then multiplicity due to the multiplicity of utterers can occur. And if along with that the personality of the utterance is also realized, i.e., only that poem which was established with the tongue of Imru' al-Qais at a specific time, then it becomes a real personal, in which multiplicity is absolutely not possible. The author's terminology is that this kind of specific composition without distinction of locus and without determination of utterance he calls a personal matter which is not subject to definition. His saying: 'And Ibn al-Hajib defined...' meaning it is apparent that Ibn al-Hajib presented this definition as a collective personal entity, not as a universal concept. The reason for the appearance is the word 'min' which is in 'minhu' because it is partitive (min al-tab'id), and the pronoun in the genitive refers to 'the revealed speech'. Then that speech of which a surah is a miracle can be a total collective, because the surah is not specified; rather all surahs are miracles, not specific to some, but the surahs of the entire Quran are all miracles. So in this respect, this definition applies to the collective as a whole. However, it could be said that it is an attempt to make a definition of the universal concept, meaning if 'surah minhu' means a surah from the Quran, not from its genre, it could be a definition of a universal concept. And circularity does not necessarily occur in any case. In the first case, when it is a definition of a collective personal, non-occurrence of circularity is apparent as the author explained. If it is a definition of a universal concept, then also circularity does not necessarily occur because we do not accept that knowledge of the concept of surah depends on knowledge of the Quran, because the concept of surah is 'a part of the revealed speech which has a beginning'.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition of the Quran, specifically whether it is a 'personal entity' (shakhsi haqiqi) or a conceptual universal (mafhum kulli). The author begins by noting that the Quran is not a real personal entity like the one that existed with Jibril (the actual speech of Allah), but rather it is whatever recitation or text is present. However, the author (presumably of the matn) said it is personal, so the commentator offers two interpretations (ta'wilan): (1) It is metaphorical: just as a real personal entity cannot be defined by a limit (hadd) because its reality is only known by pointing or specific indication, similarly the Quran's reality cannot be known except by reciting it entirely. (2) It is terminologically personal: a composition that cannot be multiplied except with a change of locus is called personal in scholarly terminology, so it is not subject to definition. Then the commentator clarifies that if the goal is not knowledge of the true reality but merely distinction from other things, then a definition is possible, e.g., 'the collection transmitted between the two covers by tawatur'. He gives examples like the definition of 'Kashshaf' or 'Nahw'. The text then discusses the Quranic example of Imru' al-Qais's poem: its specific composition makes it a unique personal entity, but multiplicity of utterers can create multiplicity. The author then states that Ibn al-Hajib's definition of Quran is actually a definition of a collective personal (majmu' shakhsi) not a universal concept, because of the partitive 'min' in his phrase 'surah minhu'. However, it could also be interpreted as a universal concept if 'min' is taken to indicate genre. The discussion ends by analyzing circularity (dawr): no circularity arises because knowledge of the concept of surah does not depend on knowledge of the Quran.

Key Terms
شخصی (shakhsi): A personal entity that cannot be multiplied without change of locus; it is not subject to definition by limits.
تحدید (tahdid): Definition by limits, which aims to describe the reality (haqiqah) of a thing but cannot capture a personal entity.
دور (dawr): Circularity in definition, where knowing the definiendum depends on knowing the definiens which itself depends on the definiendum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two interpretations (ta'wilan) for considering the Quran as a personal entity (shakhsi)?
A) It is metaphorical and it is terminological
B) It is literal and it is metaphorical
C) It is real and it is figurative
D) It is universal and it is particular

Key Takeaway
The Quran, as a personal entity, resists a true definition (hadd) that captures its essence; only recitation or indication reveals its reality, but a descriptive definition can distinguish it from other speech.

Defining the Quran: Nazm, Meaning, and Abu Hanifa's View
Clarifying the definition of Quran and resolving a historical Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why Hanafi usul scholars use nazm instead of lafz for the Quran and explain the final Hanafi position on the necessity of Arabic composition in prayer recitation.

Core Idea
The Quran is both Arabic composition (nazm) and meaning; the earlier view allowing Persian in prayer was retracted.

In defining the Quran, Hanafi usul scholars carefully choose the term nazm (composition) over lafz (word) to avoid disrespect. This lesson unpacks that choice and addresses a famous reported view of Imam Abu Hanifa that seemed to separate nazm from meaning in prayer. Understanding the retraction clarifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terminological choice
Identify why nazm is used instead of lafz for the Quran. Recognize that lafz literally means 'to throw from the mouth', which is disrespectful when applied to divine speech.
Nazm conveys composition with dignity.
2
Identify the disputed view
Learn what was reported from Abu Hanifa: that in prayer, recitation of meaning alone (even in Persian) could suffice, leading some to think nazm is not essential.
This view was specific to prayer only.
3
Analyze the clarification and limitation
See how the commentator limits Abu Hanifa's statement to the validity of prayer, while for other rulings (like prohibition for junub) Arabic nazm remains essential.
The exception was narrow: prayer recitation.
4
Confirm the retraction and final position
Understand that later authorities confirm Abu Hanifa retracted this view. The final Hanafi position requires Arabic nazm for Quranic recitation in prayer.
The matn of al-Tanqih omits the earlier view.

قال المصنف في التوضيح الباب الاول لما كان القرون نظما والا الخ چونکہ قرآن مجید نظم دال علی المعنی کا نام ہے اس لئے قسم اللفظ بالنسبه الى المعنى اربع تقسیمات لفظ کی معنی کے لحاظ سے یہ چار تقسیمات میں نظم و لفظ ایک ہی شئی ہے چونکہ قرآن مجید پر لفظ کا اطلاق کرنا اس میں ایک قسم کا سور ادب ہے کیونکہ لفظ کا لغوی مفہوم اسقاط شيء من الفم کا نام ہے. یعنی کسی شتی کو منہ سے پھینکنا قرآن مجید کے اوپر اس معنی مذکور کے لحاظ سے اطلاق کرنے میں سوم ادب تھا ۔ فلمن اختار النظم اسی وجہ سے نظم کو بجائے لفظ کے اختیار کیا وقد روی عن ابي حنيفة الخ امام ابو حنیفہ کی اس روایت مذکورہ سے یہ وہم ہوتا تھا کہ قرآن مجید صرف معنی کا نام ہے اور نظم و عبارت کو اس میں دخل نہیں ہے اس لئے اس روایت کی وضات کردی اور کہا کہ امام کے نزدیک صرف جواز صلوۃ کے بارہ میں نظم رکن لازمی نہیں ہے بلکہ انھوں نے صرف معنی کو جواز صلوۃ کے بارہ میں فریضہ قرآن کے ادا ہو جانے کے لئے کافی سمجھا ہے حتی کہ اگر کوئی شخص بغیر عربیہ میں بلا عذر نماز کے اندر قرآہ کرے تو صلوة جائز ہو جائیگی و انما قال خاصة یعنی جو از صلوۃ کی تخصیص اس لئے کی کہ نماز کے علاوہ باقی احکام میں نظم کو لازمی قرار دیا ہے کقرأة الجنب والحائض یعنی جنب اور حائض کے لئے جو قرآہ قران ممنوع ہے یہ وہی عربی النظم کی قرآة ممنوع ہے اگر قران مجید کی ایک آیت فارسی میں پڑھ لیں تو ان کے لئے جائز ہے کیونکہ یہ حقیقت قرآن نہیں کیونکہ نظم عربی اس کے ساتھ شامل نہیں ہے اس لئے فارسی لسان میں ترجمہ کو قرآن نہیں کہا جائیگا لکن الاصح لیکن صحیح یہ ہے که امام رحمہ اللہ کا اس قول سے یعنی اس سے کہ نظم بحق جواز صلوۃ رکن لازم نہیں ہے رجوع ثابت ہے اس لئے متن تنضیح میں اس قول کا ذکر نہیں کیا بل قلت بلکہ متن میں کہا که قران مجید نظیم دال علی المعنی سے عبارت و تعبیر ہے
The author said in Al-Tawdih: The first chapter — since the Qur'an is nazm (composition) indicating meaning, therefore the division of lafz (word) in relation to meaning is four divisions. Nazm and lafz are one and the same thing. Since applying the term lafz to the Qur'an involves a kind of bad manners because the lexical meaning of lafz is 'to throw something from the mouth' — applying it to the Qur'an in that sense would be disrespect. Therefore nazm was chosen instead of lafz. It is narrated from Abu Hanifa — from this narration it was misunderstood that the Qur'an is only meaning and nazm/expression has no part in it. So he clarified this narration and said that in the Imam's view, nazm is not a necessary pillar only with regard to the validity of prayer. Rather, he considered only the meaning sufficient for fulfilling the obligation of Qur'an in prayer, to the extent that if a person recites in non-Arabic without excuse in prayer, the prayer becomes valid. He specified 'validity of prayer' because in other rulings besides prayer, nazm is obligatory, like the recitation of a junub and menstruating woman — that is, the recitation of Arabic nazm is prohibited for them. If they recite an ayah of Quran in Persian, it is permissible for them because it is not the reality of Quran since Arabic nazm is not included with it. So a translation in Persian language will not be called Quran. But the correct view is that the Imam's retraction from this statement (that nazm is not a necessary pillar for the validity of prayer) is established. Therefore, in the matn of al-Tanqih, this statement is not mentioned. Rather, in the matn it is said: 'The noble Quran is an expression and interpretation of nazm indicating meaning.'
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition and clarification. It opens by explaining why the authors of usul use the term nazm (composition) instead of lafz (word) for the Quran: lafz etymologically means 'to throw out from the mouth', which is disrespectful when applied to the divine speech. Nazm is chosen for its dignity. Then it addresses a reported statement of Imam Abu Hanifa that could be misunderstood as saying the Quran is merely meaning without Arabic composition. The commentator clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa's view was limited to the validity of prayer: for the obligation of recitation in prayer, meaning alone suffices (allowing Persian translation). This was not his general view; for other rulings like the prohibition of a junub touching the Quran, the Arabic nazm is essential. However, the correct position (as per later authorities) is that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted this view. Hence, the matn of al-Tanqih defines the Quran as nazm indicating meaning, affirming that both the Arabic composition and meaning are integral.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The Arabic composition/arrangement of the Quran that indicates meaning.
لفظ (lafz): A word, literally meaning to throw from the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the status of reciting a Persian translation of the Fatiha in prayer?
A) It fulfills the obligation because meaning alone suffices.
B) It does not fulfill the obligation because Arabic nazm is essential.
C) It is permissible only for the junub and menstruating.
D) It is makruh but the prayer is valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of the Quran as nazm indicating meaning requires Arabic composition for recitation in prayer, and Imam Abu Hanifa's reported allowance was later retracted.

Proof that 'Nazm and Ma'na' Means Nazm Indicating Meaning
Providing proof from the definition of Quran and i'jaz that nazm and ma'na together means nazm indicating meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key attributes of the Qur'an used in the Hanafi proof that 'nazm and ma'na' means nazm indicating meaning, and apply this reasoning to similar textual analysis questions.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's attributes are attributes of the wording that indicates meaning, so 'nazm and ma'na' means nazm that indicates meaning.

In this passage, we examine a foundational Hanafi proof that the phrase 'nazm and ma'na together' does not mean two separate entities but rather the nazm (wording) that conveys meaning. The proof draws on the definition of the Qur'an and the nature of i'jaz (inimitability), showing that attributes like Arabic, mushaf, and tawatur belong to the wording, not the meaning alone. By working through this argument, you will sharpen your ability to analyze textual statements and understand how the Hanafi school understands the relationship between wording and meaning.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Claim
The passage aims to prove that when scholars say 'the Qur'an is nazm and ma'na together', they mean 'nazm that indicates meaning' (nazm dal 'ala al-ma'na), not two independent components.
2
2. Extract the Premises
Two premises are given: (a) The definition of the Qur'an includes attributes (Arabic, mushaf, tawatur) that are attributes of lafz (wording) indicating meaning. (b) I'jaz is a property of balaghah (eloquence), which inheres in the nazm.
Note that these premises are about the attributes of the Qur'an, not the meaning itself.
3
3. Analyze the Logical Link
If the attributes belong to wording that indicates meaning, then when we say 'nazm and ma'na', the emphasis is on nazm as the carrier and indicator of meaning. I'jaz, being a perfection of eloquence, is an attribute of nazm, not of isolated meaning.
The passage explains that i'jaz arises when composition reaches a level of eloquence that cannot be imitated.
4
4. Consider the Objection and Response
An objection claims that i'jaz is an attribute of meaning since the meanings of the Qur'an are vast. The response is that this vastness itself is due to the i'jaz of the nazm, which uniquely carries those meanings.
This shows the Hanafi view that the miracle lies in the wording's capacity to convey such depth.
5
5. Understand the Mashayikh's Context
The mashayikh used the phrase to counter the misconception that the Qur'an is only meaning (arising from a ruling on Persian recitation). Their intention was always 'nazm indicating meaning'.
This clarifies that the phrase is context-sensitive and does not imply a dual-entity view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للقطع یہ دلیل ہے اس کی کہ القران و النظم والمعنی میں مراد نظم دال على المعنیٰ ہے خلاصہ دلیل یہ کہ قرآن مجید کی تعریف میں جو کہا گیا ہے عربی ہونا اور مکتوب فی المصاحف اور منقول بالتواتر یہ تمام صفات لفظ دال علی المعنی کی ہیں اور مجموعہ لفظ و معنی کی نہیں ہیں وكذا الاعجاز اور اسی طرح اعجاز قرآن جو بلاغتہ کی ایک حد ہے متعلق بالبلاغہ ہے اور بلاغہ ان صفات میں سے ہے جن کا رجوع لفظ کی طرف ہے بایں اعتبار کہ وہ مفید معنی ہے فانه اذا قصدت یہ اس کی توضیح ہے کہ بلاغت صفہ راجعہ الی النظم الدال علی المعنی ہے یعنی جب ایک معنی کو کسی ترکیب کے ساتھ ادا کرنا مقصود ہو تو اغراض مختلفہ پیدا ہو جاتے ہیں جو مخصوص کیفیات اور خصوصیات کی مقتضی ہوتی ہیں جن کا نظم میں اعتبار کرنا ضروری ہو جاتا مثلا انکار مخاطب تاکید و مقتضی ہے اور اگر حصر کرنا مقصود ہو تو وہ تقدیم وغیرہ کی خصوصیات کو تقاضا کرتا ہے جب ان خصوصیات کی بقدر طاقت بشریہ مناسب طریقہ پر رعایت کی جائے تو کلام بلیغ ہو جاتی ہے جب ایسی حد تک پہنچ جائے کہ اس کا معارضہ نا ممکن ہو جائے تو یہ کلام معجز ہو جاتی ہے فالاعجاز تو اس صورت میں صفت نظم ہے بایں اعتبار کہ وہ مفید معنی ہو مجموعہ نظم ومعنی کی صفت نہیں ہے بہر حال قرآن مجید کے متعلق جو النظم والمعنى جميعا کہا جاتا ہے اس سے مراد النظم الدال علی المعنی ہے ۔ و قد يقال یہ اس پر معارضہ ہے کہ بلاغت صفتہ لفظ ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ قرآن کا معنی و مفہوم بنفس خود معجز ہے کہ معنی قران پر پوری اطلاع حاصل کرنا طاقتہ بشریہ سے خارج ہے كما يقال جیسے کہا جاتا ہے کہ فاتحہ کی تفسیر علم کے کئی اوقار ہیں یعنی اس کی تفسیر اتنا کثیر ہے جو بہت سے اونٹوں کا بوجھ ہو جائے گا تو معلوم ہوا کہ اعجاز صفت معنی ہے ۔ والجواب ان هذا خلاصہ جواب یہ ہے کہ یہ بھی نظم کا اعجاز ہے کہ نظم قرآن اتنا کثیر معانی کی حامل ہے کہ اس کے علاوہ دوسری عبارات و الفاظ اتنا معانی کے حامل نہیں ہو سکتے ۔ دمقصود المشائخ یعنی مشائخ نے قرآن مجید کے متعلق ھو النظم والمعنی جمیعا کیا ہے اس سے مراد نظم دال علی المعنی ہے مجموعہ نظم و معنی مراد نہیں ہے لیکن مشائخ نے یہ لفظ میں بولکر اس تو ھم کو دفع کیا جو امام کے قول بجواز القرأة الفارسیة فی الصلوۃ سے پیدا ہوتا تھا کہ قرآن صرف معنی کا نام ہے نظم کو کوئی دخل نہیں تو مشائخ نے دفع تو ھم کی خاطر النظم والمعنی جمیعا کہہ دیا اور مراد وہی نظم دال
This is a conclusive proof that in 'the Qur'an, and nazm and ma'na together', the intended meaning is nazm that indicates meaning. Summary of the proof: In the definition of the Holy Qur'an, what is said—being Arabic, written in the mushafs, transmitted by tawatur—all these are attributes of a word that indicates meaning, not of the combination of word and meaning. Similarly, i'jaz (inimitability). Likewise, the i'jaz of the Qur'an, which is a limit of its eloquence, is related to eloquence, and eloquence is among those attributes that refer to the word, in the sense that it is beneficial for meaning. For when one intends to express a meaning with a particular composition, various objectives arise that necessitate specific qualities and characteristics that must be considered in the nazm. For example, denial of the addressee necessitates emphasis, and if restriction is intended, it demands characteristics like تقدیم (preposing). When these characteristics are observed appropriately to the extent of human capability, the speech becomes eloquent. When it reaches a level that imitation is impossible, then the speech becomes miraculous. So i'jaz in that case is an attribute of nazm, in the sense that it is beneficial for meaning; it is not an attribute of the combination of nazm and meaning. In any case, regarding the Holy Qur'an, what is said 'nazm and ma'na together' means nazm that indicates meaning. It may be objected: eloquence is an attribute of word. Summary: The meaning and concept of the Qur'an itself is miraculous, such that full knowledge of the meaning of the Qur'an is beyond human capability. As it is said, the tafsir of Surah al-Fatihah has many awqar (loads of a camel), meaning its tafsir is so voluminous that it would fill many camel loads. So it becomes clear that i'jaz is an attribute of meaning. The answer: This is also the i'jaz of the nazm, because the nazm of the Qur'an carries so many meanings that other expressions and words cannot carry those meanings. And the objective of the mashayikh: They said about the Holy Qur'an 'it is nazm and meaning together'. The intended meaning from this is nazm that indicates meaning, not the combination of nazm and meaning. However, the mashayikh used this wording to remove the misconception that arose from the Imam's statement about the permissibility of reciting in Persian in prayer, i.e., that the Qur'an is only meaning, and nazm has no part. So the mashayikh said 'nazm and meaning together' to remove that misconception, and the intended meaning is still nazm that indicates.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage provides a proof (dalil) that the phrase 'nazm and ma'na' refers to nazm that indicates meaning, not two separate entities. The proof is drawn from the definition of the Qur'an: its attributes (Arabic, written in mushafs, mutawatir) are characteristics of the wording (lafz) that indicates meaning, not of the meaning alone. Similarly, the i'jaz (inimitability) of the Qur'an is attributed to its nazm because eloquence (balaghah) is a quality of the nazm that conveys meaning effectively. The passage explains that when a meaning is expressed in a specific composition, the nazm adjusts to the objective (e.g., emphasis for denial, preposing for restriction). I'jaz is then the nazm's perfection in conveying meaning. An objection is raised: some say the meaning itself is miraculous, citing that the tafsir of Surah al-Fatihah is vast. The response is that this vastness of meaning is itself due to the i'jaz of the nazm, which carries those meanings. Finally, the passage explains that the mashayikh used the expression 'nazm and meaning together' to counter the false notion that the Qur'an is only meaning (arising from the permissibility of Persian recitation in prayer according to some), while their actual intent was always 'nazm indicating meaning'. This is a classic Hanafi position: the Qur'an is both the wording and the meaning, but emphasis is on the wording as the carrier, and the meaning is inseparable.

Key Terms
إعجاز (i'jaz): The miraculous inimitability of the Qur'an, considered an attribute of the nazm that perfectly conveys meaning.
بلاغة (balaghah): Eloquence of speech, which is an attribute of the nazm that makes it effectively convey meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The passage uses the definition of the Qur'an to argue that the phrase 'nazm and ma'na together' means:
A) The Qur'an is two separate entities: wording and meaning
B) Nazm that indicates meaning
C) Only the meaning is important
D) The wording is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the attributes of the Qur'an prove that 'nazm and ma'na' means nazm indicating meaning, affirming the wording as the primary carrier of revelation.

Permissibility of Reciting Qur'anic Translation in Prayer: The Hanafi Istihsan
Explaining the rukhsa to drop nazm and rely on meaning in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi istihsan principle to derive the ruling on reciting translation in prayer and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
Meaning is primary; nazm is a means that can be dropped when the meaning is fulfilled.

The Hanafi school permits recitation of the Qur'an's meaning (translation) in prayer, based on the usul principle that the objective (maqsad) is the meaning. This lesson unpacks the derivation (istinbat) of this ruling, showing how the primary objective in prayer (munajat) justifies a concession that drops the specific wording. We will explore the conditions that govern this concession and address a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base principle
Recognize that in Hanafi usul, nazm (specific wording) is a means to convey meaning; the meaning (ma'na) is the primary objective (maqsud asli). This is derived from the statement 'lil an mabna al-nazm ala al-sa'ah wa al-was'ah...'.
2
Apply the prayer context
Prayer is munajat (private conversation with Allah), where the meaning is the essential element. Therefore, in prayer, the meaning takes precedence over the wording.
3
Understand the type of rukhsa
The concession is rukhsat isqatiyya: it drops the obligation entirely, not limited to those with an excuse. Hence, both 'excused' and 'non-excused' benefit equally.
Rukhsat isqat differs from rukhsat tafsir (reduction concession).
4
List the conditions
The concession is conditional: (1) the person is not a mubtadi' (innovator), (2) the translation is not mu'awwal (speculative interpretation), (3) order and arrangement are preserved (according to some), and (4) it is not done deliberately (according to some).
If conditions are violated, the prayer may be invalid.
5
Address the objection
The objection: if nazm is part of the definition of the Qur'an, why is it not required? The response: the 'intended pillar' (rukn) is the meaning; once meaning is conveyed, the purpose of recitation is fulfilled.

His statement: Rather, he considered the meaning, meaning that Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) rightly considered the meaning of the Qur'an for the validity of prayer, because the foundation of nazm is upon expansion and breadth, for the primary objective is the concept and meaning, and the word is only a means for conveying the meaning. Therefore, the word is secondary in intent, and the meaning is the primary objective. Especially in the state of prayer, which is a private conversation with Allah, the meaning, which is the primary objective, is considered. So in that state, there is a concession of dropping (isqat) regarding the nazm, meaning that the nazm is dropped from the responsibility of the prayer. And the concession of dropping is not specific to those with an excuse, so in this concession, the excused and the non-excused are equal. However, this concession is conditional upon specific conditions: first, that the person is not accused of innovation; second, that the word or words he utters in other than Arabic are not of the type of mu'awwal (interpreted), because an interpretative meaning is not definitive, and the Qur'an is definitive, and it should not be possible for different meanings. Therefore, any meaning expressed in Persian cannot be definitive. And it is said that some added the condition that the order (nazm) and arrangement should not be disrupted in the translation; if he starts reciting a commentary in Persian instead of literal translation, his prayer becomes invalid. And it is said: without intent, meaning that he should not do it deliberately or intentionally, but it should flow from his tongue involuntarily. And if he persists, then that person is either insane and should be treated, or a zindiq (heretic) and should be killed. As for the statement: When the Qur'an is called 'nazm indicating meaning,' then reciting the nazm is a pillar of prayer; how can it not be required for the validity of prayer? The answer: It is mentioned in the words of the later scholars that the intended pillar (the meaning) is fulfilled. Therefore, it is sufficient for the validity of prayer. Before the question: 'Rather, he considered only the meaning' is a statement. Summary of question: If the Qur'an is only the name of meaning, then it would be necessary not to consider nazm in the definition of the Qur'an, and then the definition given as 'the transmitted between the covers of the manuscripts' would also not apply to the Qur'an, because it is mutawatir, and nazm...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation) of a fiqh ruling from usul principles. It discusses the well-known Hanafi concession of reciting the meaning (translation) of the Qur'an in prayer instead of the exact Arabic text, based on the principle that meaning is the primary objective. The commentator explains that since nazm (the specific wording) is a means, it can be dropped in favor of the meaning when necessary. This concession (rukhsat isqatiyyah) applies to both the excused and the non-excused. However, conditions apply: the person must not be an innovator, the translation must not rely on speculative interpretation (mu'awwal), and according to some, the order must be preserved. Also, some hold that it should not be deliberate. If someone persists in deliberate translation, they may be treated as mad or a zindiq. The passage ends by addressing an objection: if nazm is part of the definition of the Qur'an, why is it not required? The response is that in prayer, the essential element is the meaning (ma'na), which is the real objective, hence it suffices. This definitional issue is then further questioned. This passage reflects the Hanafi approach of prioritizing the objective (maqasid) over the literal form in the context of prayer.

Key Terms
رخصة إسقاطية (rukhsa isqatiyya): A concession that drops an obligation entirely, not limited to those with an excuse.
مؤول (mu'awwal): Interpretative meaning that is not definitive; speculative interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is a condition for the permissibility of reciting translation in prayer?
A) The translation must be in classical Arabic.
B) The person must be an expert in linguistics.
C) The person must not be known for innovation and the translation must not be based on speculative interpretation.
D) The recitation must be at least three verses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the meaning is the primary objective; thus, in prayer, reciting the meaning suffices under specific conditions, reflecting the school's maqasid-oriented approach.

The Imam's Ruju' and the Non-Arabic Quran Recitation Dispute
Commentary on the imam's return to Sahibayn's opinion on Quran recitation in non-Arabic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on reciting the Quran in non-Arabic during prayer and evaluate the evidentiary weight of ruju' in this dispute.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school relies on transmitted reports of the imam's ruju' over independent reasoning to settle controversial rulings.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih and al-Talwih addresses a famous ikhtilaf: Can the Quran be recited in translation during prayer? Imam Abu Hanifah initially held that non-Arabic recitation is valid, but later reportedly returned (ruju') to the view of his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, that it invalidates the prayer. The text examines the basis of this ruju' and the interplay between transmitted report (riwayah) and juristic reasoning (istidlal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue
Recognize that the core legal question is whether Quranic recitation in prayer must be in Arabic or if non-Arabic translations suffice. The two positions are represented by Imam Abu Hanifah (permissibility) and the Sahibayn (invalidity).
The dispute is not about the merit of Arabic, but about the definition of 'Quran' and its conditions in prayer.
2
Examine the Imam's Pro-Quranic Dalil
The verse from Surah ash-Shu'ara (26:196) is cited: 'Indeed it is in the scriptures of the former peoples.' The argument is that since the earlier scriptures were non-Arabic, the term 'Quran' can apply to non-Arabic texts. Classify this dalil: what is its textual category and how does it support permissibility?
This verse is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah, opening room for interpretation.
3
Analyze the Concept of Ruju'
The commentator states that the most correct view is that the Imam returned to the Sahibayn's opinion. Understand that ruju' is a change of position by a mujtahid, often based on stronger evidence or reflection. In this case, the ruju' is established by transmitted report (riwayah) from reliable narrators, not by the cited verse alone.
Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi emphasized that if ruju' is proven by transmission, independent proof (istidlal) is unnecessary.
4
Assess the Methodological Critique
The text presents a critique: the verse used by Abu Hanifah does not actually constitute a proof for the return because it supports his original position, not the reversal. Abu al-Yusr and al-Karkhi found the matter deeply problematic. Determine what this critique reveals about the hierarchy of evidence in Hanafi usul.
The critique highlights that reliance on transmitted reports (riwayah) of a mujtahid's final position outweighs the apparent meaning of textual proofs.
5
Derive the Mufta Bihi Position
Conclude that the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that non-Arabic recitation invalidates the prayer. This is based on the Sahibayn's view, reaffirmed by the Imam's ruju', and supported by the principle that the final position of a mujtahid is authoritative.
The practical consequence: a person who prays using a Quran translation must repeat the prayer in Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چونکہ قائم اور موجود ہے اس لئے احتیاطاً اس پر احکام قرآن مترتب کر دئیے ۔ قوله لكن الاصح لیکن اصح اور زیادہ صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ امام نے اس میں رجوع الی قول الصاحبین کیا ہے یعنی قرآة القرآن بغیر العربیہ نماز کے جواز کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے یہی وایت کیا ہے نوح بن مریم ابو عصمہ قاضی المروني . قال فخر الاسلام لان ما قالہ یہ وجہ رجوع کا بیان ہے استدلال علی الرجوع نہیں ہے کیونکہ اگر رجوع ثابت بالروایت ہے تو پھر استدلال کی ضرورت نہیں ہے رجوع ثابت نہیں ہے تو یہ استدلال مفید مقصود نہیں ہے کیونکہ قران مجید کی وصف جیسے منزل عربی کے ساتھ ہے ایسے غیر عربی کے ساتھ کیگئی ہے جیسے انہ لفی تو بر الاولین تو زبر اول میں اس کا وجود ثابت ہو رہا ہے یہ عربی لسان میں نہ تھا بلکہ غیر عربی میں تھا تو معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن کا اطلاق غیر عربی پر بھی ہو سکتا ہے۔ وقال ابو الیسر ابوالیسر فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ مسئلہ بڑا مشکل ہے اور خارج از فہم کیونکہ ابو حنیفہ کا یہ قول کسی کے لئے واضح نہیں ہو سکتا۔ کرخی نے اس مسئلہ میں ایک تصنیف طویل لکھی ہے لیکن دلیل شافی اس میں بھی نہیں ہے۔
Because it is established and present, therefore precautionarily the rulings of the Quran are applied to it. His saying: 'but the most correct' means the more correct and sound view is that the Imam in this matter returned to the opinion of the two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), i.e., reciting the Quran without Arabic is not sufficient for the validity of the prayer. This is what Nu'ah ibn Maryam, Abu 'Ismah al-Qadi al-Marwani narrated. Fakhral-Islam said: Because what he said is an expression of the reason for the return, not a proof for the return. For if the return is established by transmission, then there is no need for proof; if the return is not established, then this proof is not useful for the intended purpose. Because the description of the Noble Quran, just as it is revealed in Arabic, similarly it has been made in non-Arabic, like the verse 'Indeed it is in the scriptures of the former peoples.' Its existence is established in the first scriptures; it was not in the Arabic tongue but in non-Arabic. So it becomes known that the term 'Quran' can be applied to non-Arabic as well. And Abu al-Yusr said: Abu al-Yusr states that this matter is very difficult and beyond comprehension, because this statement of Abu Hanifah cannot be made clear to anyone. Al-Karkhi wrote a lengthy work on this matter, but even that does not contain a satisfactory proof.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of Imam Abu Hanifah's reported return (ruju') to the opinion of his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the permissibility of reciting the Quran in non-Arabic during prayer. The commentator explains that the most correct view is that the Imam indeed returned, making the recitation in non-Arabic insufficient for prayer's validity. Fakhral-Islam (al-Bazdawi) then clarifies that the Imam's statement merely explains the reason for the return, not a proof (istidlal) establishing the return. If the return is proven by transmitted report (riwayah), reasoning is superfluous; if not, reasoning fails. The proof cited is that the Quran is described as being in the 'zubur al-awwalin' (scriptures of the former peoples), which were in non-Arabic, showing the term 'Quran' can apply to non-Arabic. Abu al-Yusr finds the issue intractable, and Al-Karkhi's treatise on the subject is noted as lacking a satisfactory proof. The discussion highlights the complexity of the Hanafi position and the need to rely on transmitted reports over independent reasoning.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): The imam's adoption of a previously held opinion by his disciples, abandoning his own initial ruling.
صاحبين (al-sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the primary students of Abu Hanifah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He absolutely permitted it without exception.
B) He prohibited it entirely.
C) He initially permitted it but later returned to the view of the Sahibayn that it is not valid.
D) He allowed it only if the person cannot speak Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's final ruling on non-Arabic Quran recitation in prayer is invalidity, based on the authentic report of Imam Abu Hanifah's ruju' to the Sahibayn's position.
