Hanafi Proof that Khul' is a Type of Talaq
Deriving the Hanafi position that khul' is talaq from Quranic wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi istinbat method to derive the ruling that khul' is a type of talaq from Quran 2:230, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Quranic 'fa' in 'fain talaqaha' proves khul' is talaq — annulment would render the sequence meaningless.

The legal nature of khul' (divorce initiated by wife with compensation) is disputed between Hanafis and Shafi'is. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof from the Quran, focusing on the particle 'fa' in verse 2:230 and its linguistic-legal implications. You will learn how a single letter can determine a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text
Locate the Quranic verses cited: 2:229 ('al-talaqu marratan' and permission for khul') and 2:230 ('fain talaqaha'). Note the word 'fa' connecting them.
The full passage from the source chunk covers both verses.
2
Analyze the particle 'fa'
Determine the linguistic function of 'fa' in 'fain talaqaha'. In Hanafi usul, 'fa' indicates immediate succession ('ta'qib'). This is the core of the argument.
The source calls it 'fa' al-ta'qibiyya'.
3
Apply the contextual sequence
Observe the order: (1) two revocable divorces, (2) permission for khul', (3) 'then if he divorces her...' If khul' were an annulment, no further divorce could occur, making the 'fa' contradictory.
This is the Hanafi inference.
4
Derive the ruling
Since a divorce after khul' is possible, khul' cannot completely dissolve the marriage. Hence, khul' is a type of talaq (revocable divorce with compensation), not faskh.
Final Hanafi position.
5
Compare the Shafi'i reading
The Shafi'i school interprets 'fa' as a mere narrative connector and holds khul' is faskh. Identify the practical difference (thamarah): after khul', Hanafis allow a revocable divorce; Shafi'is consider the marriage ended.
Source calls Shafi'i view 'addition to the Book'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And His saying, exalted is He: 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him afterwards.' The 'fa' is a specific word, i.e., the word 'fa' is a specific particle whose usage is for immediate succession. And He has attached (the divorce) after mentioning the divorce, i.e., Allah first mentioned divorce (the two revocable divorces), and after that mentioned khul' (fida), and after this khul' He said 'fain talaqaha' (then if he divorces her), so the 'fa' is for succession. So according to its specific meaning, it necessitates that after khul', a clear divorce can occur. If a clear divorce cannot occur after khul', as is the madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i, then the requirement of the specific word, i.e., the 'fa' of succession, would be nullified, which is not correct. The verification of this issue is detailed as follows: Allah mentioned divorce that is revocable, i.e., the divorce that is two times, by His saying 'al-talaqu marratan' (divorce is twice) and then mentioned the woman's fida: 'fala junaha 'alayhima fima iftadat bihi' (there is no sin upon them in what she ransoms herself with). In 'fima iftadat', Allah mentioned only the woman's action, i.e., she gives financial compensation, and He did not mention the husband's action. So in it, the husband's action is implicit from what preceded and is established clearly; his action in what preceded is divorcing. So it is as if Allah mentioned two types of divorce: one without compensation, which is 'al-talaqu marratan', and the second type, divorce with compensation, which He mentioned in His saying 'fala junaha 'alayhima fima iftadat bihi'. From this it is understood that khul' is a type of divorce, not an annulment of marriage. Whoever understands it as annulment, like Imam al-Shafi'i, that would be an addition to the Book of Allah, because the word 'iftida' does not indicate annulment. So taking the meaning of annulment is adding from oneself. In contrast, interpreting it as divorce, as is the madhhab of the Hanafis, no addition to the Book is necessary because divorce is already mentioned in the first part of the speech; it is the husband's action, and no addition from oneself is needed. Then He said 'fain talaqaha' i.e., after the two times, i.e., after this fida, He said 'fain talaqaha' meaning if he divorces her after the two times, whether they are without compensation or with compensation, then 'fala tahillu lahu' (she is not lawful for him). So 'fain talaqaha' is...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi istinbat (derivation) that khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation) is a type of talaq, not a faskh (annulment). The key evidence is the Quranic phrase 'fain talaqaha' (then if he divorces her) which appears after the verse on khul'. The particle 'fa' indicates immediate succession, showing that a divorce can occur after khul'. If khul' were an annulment, the marriage would be completely dissolved and no further divorce could occur, rendering the 'fa' meaningless. Additionally, the verse 'al-talaqu marratan' (divorce is twice) is followed by the permission for khul', implying that khul' is a type of divorce (with compensation) that replaces one of the revocable divorces. The Hanafi argues that interpreting khul' as faskh requires adding a meaning to the Quran that is not indicated by the word 'iftida', which is an illegitimate addition. The passage is a clear example of Hanafi reliance on Quranic context and linguistic analysis to derive legal rulings.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife by paying compensation to the husband.
فاء التعقيبية (fa' al-ta'qibiyya): The particle 'fa' indicating immediate succession or sequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key Quranic evidence the Hanafi uses to prove that khul' is a type of talaq?
A) The word 'talaq' appears in the khul' verse.
B) The phrase 'fain talaqaha' (then if he divorces her) after the khul' verse.
C) A hadith stating khul' is talaq.
D) Consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school proves khul' is a type of talaq through the Quranic 'fa' of immediate succession, a classic example of usul al-fiqh in action.

Proof of Mahr Obligation from the Letter Ba'
Using the literal meaning of the letter ba' to prove that mahr is due upon marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Hanafi use of the letter ba' in Quran 4:24 to derive the immediate obligation of mahr and differentiate it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The letter ba' in 'bi amwalikum' means attachment, proving mahr is integral to the marriage contract itself.

This lesson examines a key Quranic proof used by Hanafi usulis to establish that the mahr (dower) becomes obligatory immediately upon a valid marriage contract, not later. At the heart of the argument is the Arabic letter ba' and its literal meaning of ilsaq (attachment). Understanding this derivation is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on the mufawwidah case—where a woman marries without specifying mahr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic text
Locate the phrase 'an tabtaghu bi amwalikum' in Quran 4:24. This is the proof text (dalil).
The verse is about seeking marriage through wealth.
2
Define the letter ba'
According to Hanafi usul, the ba' in 'bi amwalikum' is a specific word (lafz khaas) with a literal meaning of attachment (ilsaq). Confirm that this is the haqiqah (real meaning) of ba'.
The ba' is not simply a preposition; it carries substantive legal meaning.
3
Apply the meaning to the contract
Since ba' indicates attachment, the act of seeking (tabtaghu), which refers to the marriage contract, is attached to wealth (amwal). Therefore, wealth (mahr) is essential to the contract.
This implies mahr becomes wajib upon the contract itself, not later.
4
Contrast with invalid contract
The passage notes that in a fasid (invalid) marriage, mahr is not wajib by contract but by consummation. This boundary clarifies the rule for valid contracts.
The distinction reinforces that the ba' proof applies only to valid contracts.
5
Apply to the mufawwidah case
For a woman who marries without mahr (mufawwidah), the Hanafis rule that her marriage is valid and mahr becomes wajib upon contract, eventually determined as mahr al-mithl. Shafi'is disagree, holding the marriage invalid without mahr.
This practical difference stems directly from the interpretation of ba'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The degree of our reliance is mentioned in the text and explained, so there is no need to repeat. And His saying 'that you seek with your wealth' – the ba' is a specific word, meaning the ba' in 'bi amwalikum' is a specific word whose placement is for attachment (ilsaq), and this is the real meaning of ba'. And 'tabtaghu' means seeking by contract, not seeking by hire or by mut'ah, and a valid contract is intended. Therefore, the requirement of ba' is that the valid marriage contract is attached to wealth, and it can never be separated from wealth. So from this verse, it is known that wealth, i.e., the right of mahr, becomes obligatory with the very contract, not dependent on the guardian's permission. Unlike the invalid contract: when the marriage is with an invalid contract, the right of mahr is not obligatory with the contract itself, but rather with intercourse and consummation. This is in opposition to al-Shafi'i: Imam al-Shafi'i does not hold that it is obligatory with the contract itself. And the difference here is the case of al-mufawwidah: the word mufawwidah is derived from tafwid, and is used for marriage without mention of mahr and marriage on condition of no mahr. Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the Quranic verse 'an tabtaghu bi amwalikum' (that you seek with your wealth). The author argues that the letter ba' in 'bi amwalikum' has a literal meaning of 'ilsaq' (attachment), indicating that wealth (mahr) is attached to the marriage contract itself. Therefore, mahr becomes obligatory immediately upon a valid marriage contract, not conditional on consummation or guardian's consent. This is the Hanafi position, in contrast to the Shafi'i school which holds that mahr is not obligatory by the contract alone. The passage then introduces the case of 'mufawwidah' (a woman who marries without specifying mahr), which is a practical application: Hanafis consider her marriage valid without mahr, but mahr becomes obligatory later (by consumption or separation), whereas Shafi'is have a different ruling. The text is cut off, but the point is to show the effect of the ba' derivation.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba'): The letter ba' meaning attachment (ilsaq) in the Quranic context.
المفوضة (al-mufawwidah): A woman who marries without specifying mahr or on condition of no mahr.

Key Takeaway
The letter ba' in 'bi amwalikum' is interpreted by Hanafis as ilsaq (attachment), making mahr an inseparable part of the marriage contract and wajib immediately upon a valid marriage.

Deriving the Immediate Obligation of Mahr from Quran 4:24: A Hanafi Istinbat
Hanafi textual analysis of the particle ba to derive immediate mahr obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of literal interpretation of particles to derive the immediate obligation of mahr from Quran 4:24 and contrast it with the Shafi'i reading.

Core Idea
The letter ba in 'بأموالكم' means attachment; thus mahr is attached to the marriage contract itself.

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school derives the ruling that mahr (dower) becomes obligatory immediately upon concluding a valid marriage contract. The key is the particle 'ba' in the Quranic verse 'أَن تَبۡتَغُواْ بِأَمۡوَٰلِكُمۡ' (that you seek wives with your wealth). By analyzing the literal meaning of this particle and the context of 'ibtigha' (seeking), we uncover a powerful example of Hanafi usul al-fiqh in action. This derivation also highlights a cross-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative particle
Locate the letter 'ba' in the verse and note that it is a particle (harf). The author calls it a 'particular word' (لفظ خاص).
The particle is attached to the word 'amwal' (wealth).
2
Determine its literal meaning
Apply the Hanafi principle that particles have fixed literal meanings. Here, 'ba' is for attachment (الصاق). This is its haqiqah (true meaning).
Other possible meanings like instrument or cause are not the primary sense according to Hanafis.
3
Combine with the context of 'ibtigha'
Understand that 'ibtigha' refers to seeking marriage through a valid contract, not hiring or temporary marriage. The attachment of 'ba' thus links the contract to wealth.
This restricts the interpretation to a valid nikah contract.
4
Derive the ruling of immediate obligation
Since the contract is attached to wealth, the mahr becomes inseparable from the contract itself. Therefore, it is wajib immediately upon the contract, regardless of consummation.
Contrast with an invalid contract (fasid), where mahr is only due upon actual consummation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى ان تبتغوا باموالكم الباء لفظ خاص الخ یعنی باموالکم کی با لفظ خاص ہے جس کی وضع الصاق کے لئے ہے اور یہ با کا حقیقی معنی ہے اور ابتغا سے مراد طلب بالعقد ہے نہ کہ طلب بالاجارہ اور نہ بالمتعہ اور عقد صحیح مراد ہے لہذا با کا مقتضا یہ ہے کہ نکاح کا عقد صحیح ملصق بالمال ہے اور مال سے کبھی منفک نہیں ہو سکتا تو اس آیت سے معلوم ہوا کہ مال یعنی حق المھر نفس عقد کے ساتھ ہی واجب ہو جاتی ہے موقوف علی الولی نہیں ہے بخلاف الفاسد یعنی جب نکاح بعقد فاسد ہو تو اس میں حق المہر نفس عقد کے ساتھ واجب نہیں بلکہ وطی اور دخول کے ساتھ واجب ہوتی ہے خلاف ا للشافعی
And Allah's saying 'that you seek (wives) with your wealth' – the letter ba in 'biamwalikum' is a particular word which is placed for adhesion (ilisq), and this is the real meaning of ba. And by 'ibtigha' is meant seeking through a contract, not through hiring or temporary marriage, and the valid contract is intended. Therefore, the requirement of ba is that the valid marriage contract is attached to wealth and can never be separated from wealth. So from this verse it is known that wealth, i.e., the right of mahr, becomes obligatory with the very contract, not dependent on the guardian, unlike the invalid marriage, for when marriage is with an invalid contract, the right of mahr is not obligatory with the contract itself but becomes obligatory with sexual intercourse and consummation – contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation of a legal ruling) from the Quranic verse 'أَن تَبۡتَغُواْ بِأَمۡوَٰلِكُمۡ' (that you seek [wives] with your wealth). The author analyzes the letter 'باء' in 'بأموالكم' as a 'harf khas' (a specific particle) that indicates attachment (الصاق). He argues that the literal meaning of ba is adhesion, and when applied to the marriage contract, it signifies that the mahr (dower) is attached to the contract itself, not to anything else. Therefore, as soon as a valid marriage contract is concluded, the mahr becomes obligatory, regardless of consummation. This is in contrast to Shafi'i who, according to the text, does not consider mahr obligatory at the time of the contract. The author further distinguishes between a valid contract (عقد صحیح) and an invalid one (عقد فاسد): in an invalid marriage, mahr is only obligatory upon actual consummation. This derivation demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle of adhering to the literal meaning of particles (حروف) in istinbat.

Key Terms
باء الصاق (ba al-ilsaq): The letter 'ba' used for attachment, meaning something is attached or connected to something else.
ابتغاء (ibtigha): Seeking; in this context, seeking marriage through a valid contract, not through hire or temporary marriage.
لفظ خاص (lafz khas): A specific word or particle with a defined lexical meaning; here referring to the particle ba.
وضع (wad'): The original coinage or assignment of a word/particle to a meaning in the Arabic language.
عقد صحیح (aqd sahih): A valid marriage contract that fulfills all conditions and pillars according to Sharia.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only upon consummation.
B) Immediately upon the contract.
C) Only if the guardian agrees.
D) Upon divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the immediate obligation of mahr from the literal meaning of the particle 'ba' in Quran 4:24, demonstrating the usul principle that particles have fixed meanings guiding legal derivation.

Deriving the Minimum Mahr: The Lawgiver's Exclusive Determination
Deriving from the Quran that the minimum mahr is set by the Lawgiver, not by spouses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of ascription (isnad) to derive that the minimum mahr is set by the Lawgiver, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The word 'fard' ascribed to Allah proves the minimum mahr is divinely ordained, not left to spouses.

This lesson explores a classic usul al-fiqh derivation (istinbat) from the Quranic verse 'قَدۡ عَلِمۡنَا مَا فَرَضۡنَا' (We have known what We have prescribed). The Hanafis argue that the word 'fard' (prescribed), when attributed to Allah, indicates that the determination of mahr is exclusively His domain. This leads to the ruling that the minimum mahr is fixed by the Lawgiver, not by the spouses. We will follow the reasoning step-by-step and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, which leaves the amount to mutual agreement.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Operative Term
Identify the key word 'فرض' (fard) in the Quranic verse. Understand that in this context, it means 'determination' (تقدير) rather than merely 'obligation'.
This lexical choice is critical for the Hanafi argument.
2
Analyze the Ascription (Isnad)
Note that the verb 'فرضنا' (We prescribed) attributes the act of prescribing to Allah. In Hanafi usul, ascription to the Lawgiver makes the ruling specific (khas) and exclusive to Him.
The ascription supports the idea that the determination of the mahr's minimum is not delegated to humans.
3
Derive the Intermediate Conclusion
From the specificity of the ascribed term, conclude that the determination of the mahr (its minimum) is entirely in the hands of the Lawgiver. This refutes the view that spouses may set any amount arbitrarily.
This is the core legal inference (istinbat) from the verse.
4
Apply Qiyas to Determine the Numeric Value
Since the Quran does not explicitly state the minimum amount, the Hanafis resort to qiyas. They analogize the mahr to a case where the Law has set a minimum value: the penalty for theft (amputation of a hand) which requires a stolen good worth ten dirhams. Thus, the minimum mahr is set at ten dirhams.
The Shafi'is reject this qiyas, arguing that the verse does not impose a fixed quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى قد علمنا ما فرضنا انس آیت کے اندر فرض بمعنی تقدیر کے ہے اور یہ لفظ خاص ہے اس سے یہ ثابت ہوا کہ فرض مر مختص بالشارع ہے کیونکہ فرضنا میں اسناد فرض الی الشارع کی گئی ہے تو لفظ فرض بحیثیت این که مشتمل علی الاسناد ہے خاص ہے تو اس سے قطعا ثابت ہو جائیگا کہ تقدیر مہر مختص بالشارع ہے فيكون أدناه ای اونی المهر مقدرا من الشارع اور اس ادنی مہر کی تقدیر رائے زوجین پر موقوف نہیں ہوگی خلافاته امام شافعیہ کا اس میں اختلاف ہے وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مہر کی کوئی مقدار مقرر من الشارع نہیں ہے بلکہ رائے زوجین پر موقوف ہے جو چاہیں مقرر کر لیں احناف کہتے ہیں چونکہ فرض و تقدیر کی اسناد الی الشارع ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ تقدیر مهر مفوض الى الشارع ہے اور مہر کی مقدار اعلیٰ اور اکثر تو بالاجماع کوئی مقدر من الشارع ہے۔ دوعالم ببین پھر چونکہ یہ مفروض شریعہ میں صراحة مبتین اور مذکور نہیں ہے اس لئے قدرناه بالرأى والقياس یعنی ہم نے اس کی تقدیر رائے اور قیاس کی ساتھ کی ہے ۔ والقیاس بشی یعنی ایسی شئی پر قیاس کیا جو شرعا اس باب میں معتبر ہے یعنی عضو انسانی کے عوض ہونے میں
And Allah's saying 'قد علمنا ما فرضنا' (We have known what We have prescribed). In the verse, 'فَرَضَ' is in the meaning of 'قَدَّرَ' (to determine). And this word is a specific word. From this it is proved that prescription (farad) is exclusive to the Lawgiver, because in 'فَرَضْنَا' , the ascription of prescription to the Lawgiver is made. So the word 'فرض' due to being accompanied by the ascription is specific, so it definitively proves that the determination of mahr is exclusive to the Lawgiver. Therefore, its minimum, i.e., the minimum of mahr, is set by the Lawgiver. And the estimation of this minimum mahr is not dependent on the opinion of the spouses, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i and his followers. They say that there is no specific amount of mahr determined by the Lawgiver; rather it is based on the spouses' opinion; they may set whatever they wish. The Hanafis say: Since 'prescription' and 'determination' are ascribed to the Lawgiver, it is known that the determination of mahr is delegated to the Lawgiver. And the maximum and the majority amount of mahr are not determined by the Lawgiver by consensus. But since this prescribed amount is not explicitly stated in the Law, we have determined it by opinion and qiyas. We made qiyas on something that is considered by the Law in this chapter, i.e., in the compensation for a human limb.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an istinbat (derivation) from the Quranic verse 'قد علمنا ما فرضنا' (We have known what We have prescribed). The author argues that the word 'فرض' (prescribed) implies that the determination of the dower's minimum is the exclusive domain of the Lawgiver (Allah). This is because the verb 'فرضنا' attributes the act of prescribing to Allah, making the concept of 'فرض' specific to Him. Thus, the minimum mahr is fixed by divine decree, not left to the spouses. The Hanafis, relying on this, establish a minimum mahr (ten dirhams) through qiyas on something the Law considers as compensation (like the amputation of a limb — referring to the penalty for theft). In contrast, the Shafi'is hold that there is no fixed minimum; the spouses may agree on any amount. This dispute shows how usul principles (specificity of language, attribution to the Lawgiver) lead to different fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
فرض (fard (here in the sense of fard)): In this verse, it means 'determination' (تقدير) of the mahr, and due to ascription to Allah, it indicates a fixed amount set by the Lawgiver.
ادنی المهر (adna al-mahr): The minimum mahr set by the Lawgiver, which is ten dirhams according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do the Hanafis determine the minimum amount of mahr (ten dirhams)?
A) From a clear verse or hadith specifying ten dirhams.
B) Through qiyas (analogy) on a matter that the Law considers, such as compensation for a limb.
C) By the consensus of the companions.
D) By leaving it to the couple to agree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi minimum mahr of ten dirhams is derived from a qiyas based on the divine ascription in the verse, establishing that the Lawgiver, not spouses, determines the baseline.

Refuting the Claim of Homonymy: Sajdah as Submission
Analyzing Quran 22:18 to refute homonymy of sajdah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a Quranic verse to refute a claim of linguistic homonymy (ishtirak) by examining the scope of the term and the context of 'many of the people'.

Core Idea
A single word can apply universally if its meaning is broad; 'sajdah' as submission includes both rational and non-rational beings.

In this passage, the author tackles a common argument for homonymy: that the word 'sajdah' must have two meanings because it is attributed to both rational and non-rational beings in the same verse. We will see how the author defends the single-meaning theory by showing that submission (inqiyad) is not universal among rational beings, thus preserving consistency without needing multiple meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that 'sajdah' in Quran 22:18 has two meanings: submission for non-rational beings and prostration for humans, because the phrase 'many of the people' suggests not all humans prostrate, implying a different meaning for non-rational beings.
This is a classic argument for ishtirak based on contextual inconsistency.
2
State the author's first response
The author counters that if sajdah meant submission, then 'many of the people' is appropriate because submission is not universal—disbelievers, especially the arrogant, do not submit. Therefore, no homonymy is needed; a single meaning of submission works for all.
This refutes the opponent's premise that submission would include all people.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The opponent assumes that submission includes all people, but the author shows this assumption is false. By demonstrating that submission is not universal, the opponent's key premise collapses.
This is a straightforward logical refutation.
4
Consider the alternative reading
The author also suggests that sajdah could mean literal prostration for all, but that is addressed in the next lesson. Here, the submission interpretation is defended as coherent.
The author provides two possible single-meaning interpretations, both avoiding homonymy.

وتمسكوا أيضاً بقوله تعالى ألم تر أن الله يسجد له من في السموات ومن في الأرض والشمس والقمر والنجوم والجبال والشجر والدواب وكثير من الناس وكثير حق عليه العذاب . في هذه الآية لفظ السجود واحد نسب إلى العقلاء وإلى غير العقلاء. إنما نسب إلى غير العقلاء فمنهم المراد الانقياد والطاعة وضع الجبهة على الأرض ليس مرادا، ومنسوب إلى العقلاء فمنهم المراد وضع الجبهة على الأرض. فإن قوله تعالى وكثير من الناس يدل على أن السجود الذي نسب إلى الإنسان هو وضع الجبهة على الأرض لأنه لو كان الانقياد هو المراد لكان كثير من الناس ليس مناسبا لأن الانقياد شامل لجميع الناس. فحينئذ لفظ السجود مشترك بين المعنيين. أقول تمسكهم بهذه الآية لا يتم لأنه يمكن أن يكون هنا السجود بمعنى الانقياد الذي يشمل العقلاء وغير العقلاء من نجوم وأشجار وجبال وغيرهم، وما ذكروا من أنه لو كان الانقياد مرادا لكان كثير من الناس لا ينبغي لأن الانقياد شامل لجميع الناس باطل لأن الانقياد ليس شاملا لجميع الناس لأن الكفار خصوصا المتكبرين منهم لا ينقادون، فهم خارجون عن الانقياد، فبقي كثير من الناس منقادين. وأيضا لا يبعد أن يكون المراد وضع الرأس على الأرض الذي يشمل العقلاء وغير العقلاء. وأما استحالة وضع الرأس على الأرض من الجمادات فلا يحكم باستحالته إلا من يستحيل التسبيح من الجمادات والشهادة من الجوارح والأعضاء، مع أن القرآن محكم تنزيله أتم بناطقه أن من شيء إلا يسبح بحمده، وتشهد أيديهم بما كانوا يكسبون، ناطقان بما قلنا. وقد صح أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سمع تسبيح الحصى والحجارة، وقوله تعالى ولكن لا تفقهون تسبيحهم يدل على أن تسبيح الجمادات حقيقي، لا مجرد دلالة على الوحدانية، لأن تلك الدلالة مفهومة لنا، فلا يناسب نفي الفقه. فعلم من هنا أن السجود بمعنى وضع الرأس خضوعا لله تعالى لا يمتنع من الجمادات، بل هو كائن وموجود، وإنكاره إنما يكون ممن ينكر خوارق العادات من الله تعالى.
And they also cling to the verse: 'Do you not see that Allah is prostrated to by whoever is in the heavens and whoever is on the earth, and the sun, the moon, the stars, the mountains, the trees, the beasts, and many of the people, but many are deserving of punishment.' In this verse, the word 'sajdah' is one, attributed to rational beings and non-rational beings. As for the attribution to non-rational beings, what is meant is submission and obedience, not placing the forehead on the ground. And as for the attribution to rational beings, what is meant is placing the forehead on the ground. Then His statement 'and many of the people' indicates that the sajdah attributed to humans is placing the forehead on the ground, because if submission were meant, then 'many of the people' would not be appropriate, since submission includes all people. Therefore, the word 'sajdah' is shared between the two meanings. I say: their clinging to this verse is incomplete, because it is possible that here sajdah means submission, which includes rational and non-rational beings from stars, trees, mountains, etc. And what they mentioned—that if submission were meant, then 'many of the people' should not be because submission includes all people—is false, because submission does not include all people, since the disbelievers, especially the arrogant among them, are not submissive; they are excluded from submission, so many of the people are indeed submissive. Also, it is not unlikely that what is meant is placing the head on the ground, which includes rational and non-rational beings. As for the impossibility of placing the head on the ground by inanimate objects, one cannot rule it impossible except one who deems impossible the tasbih (glorification) of inanimate objects and the testimony of limbs and organs, even though the Quran, with its firm verses, speaks that 'there is nothing that does not exalt His praise' and 'their hands will testify against them' are fully explicit in what we have said. And it is established that the Prophet, peace be upon him, heard the tasbih of pebbles and stones. And Allah's statement 'but you do not understand their tasbih' indicates that the tasbih of inanimate objects is real, not merely indicating monotheism, because that indication would be understandable to us, so negating understanding would not be appropriate. From this it is known that sajdah in the sense of placing the head in humility to Allah is not impossible for inanimate objects; rather, it occurs and exists, and its denial is only by one who denies supernatural events from Allah.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of Quran 22:18, specifically addressing whether the word 'sajdah' has two different meanings (submission for non-rational beings and prostration for rational beings) or one. The opponents argue that since the word is applied to both, it must be mushtarak (homonymous). The author refutes this in two ways: first, the verse can be interpreted consistently as 'submission' for all, and the objection that 'many of the people' would be inappropriate is invalid because not all people are submissive (disbelievers, especially the arrogant, are not). Second, it is not far-fetched that 'sajdah' means placing the head on the ground for all, including inanimate objects, because Allah has the power to do all things. The author supports this with Quranic evidence of tasbih (glorification) of inanimate objects and sound hadith of the Prophet hearing pebbles glorifying Allah. Thus, the claim of homonymy is refuted, and sajdah is proven to have a single meaning.

Key Terms
سجود (sujood): Prostration or submission; argued to be a single concept applicable to all beings
انقیاد (inqiyad): Submission, compliance; one proposed meaning of sajdah for non-rational beings
وضع الرأس على الأرض (wad' al-ra's 'ala al-ard): Placing the head on the ground; the literal prostration
تسبیح (tasbih): Glorification, exaltation of Allah; argued to be literal for inanimate objects as supporting evidence for literal sajdah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that 'many of the people' in the sajdah verse would be inappropriate if sajdah meant submission for all?
A) He says that 'many' includes all believing humans.
B) He says that submission is not universal because disbelievers, especially the arrogant, are not submissive.
C) He says that the verse actually means prostration, not submission.
D) He says that 'many' is used metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
The claim of homonymy for 'sajdah' fails because the premise that submission is universal among humans is false; a single meaning of submission is coherent across all beings in the verse.

Literal Sajdah for Inanimate Objects: Evidence from Tasbih
Using evidence of literal tasbih to argue for literal sajdah of inanimate objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite Quranic and hadith evidence to demonstrate that literal prostration of inanimate objects is possible, and to apply this to refute the claim of impossibility.

Core Idea
If inanimate objects can literally glorify Allah, they can also literally prostrate by Allah's power.

The author now addresses the second part of the opponent's argument: that literal prostration is impossible for inanimate objects. Using Quranic verses that affirm literal tasbih (glorification) of all things, and a sound hadith where the Prophet heard pebbles glorifying Allah, the author shows that Allah can cause any creation to perform physical acts. This refutes the claim of impossibility and supports the single-meaning interpretation of sajdah as literal prostration for all beings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's second argument
The opponent claims that literal prostration (placing the head on the ground) is impossible for inanimate objects like mountains, trees, and stars, so the word must mean submission for them.
This argument relies on a naturalistic assumption of impossibility.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence for literal tasbih
The author cites the verse 'there is nothing that does not exalt His praise' (Quran 17:44) and 'their hands will testify against them' (Quran 36:65) to show that inanimate objects can perform real acts of glorification and testimony.
The author emphasizes that these acts are literal, not metaphorical, because the Quran negates understanding of their tasbih, implying it is a real act beyond our perception.
3
Incorporate the hadith evidence
The author mentions the sound hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) heard pebbles and stones glorifying Allah. This confirms that inanimate objects can literally perform tasbih.
This hadith is a direct sensory evidence for the possibility of literal acts by inanimates.
4
Draw the conclusion for sajdah
If tasbih can be literal for inanimate objects, then there is no inherent impossibility for sajdah (prostration) to also be literal. The author asserts that the denial of such miracles stems from rejecting Allah's power to cause them.
The single-meaning interpretation of sajdah as literal prostration is thus vindicated.

The Part-Whole Relationship: Interpreting 'Yawm' as Absolute Time
Explaining that 'yawm' can refer to absolute time via part-whole with 'an'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a general term (amm) can be interpreted contextually to include all parts, using the part-whole relationship between 'an' and 'yawm'.

Core Idea
Yawm in oaths and rulings can mean absolute time, not just daytime, due to the part-whole link with the absolute moment 'an'.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, words are interpreted based on context and linguistic relationships. This lesson examines how the word 'yawm' (day) in the Quranic verse on battlefield desertion is understood to include night as well, because 'yawm' is composed of moments ('an') and the ruling applies to the whole. We will trace the reasoning from the Arabic text to the legal implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key word and its literal meaning
The word 'yawm' literally means 'day' as in the period from sunrise to sunset. However, the passage argues that it can also encompass night when used in certain contexts.
Literal meaning is the starting point but not the end.
2
Locate the Quranic verse and the word in context
The verse is Quran 8:16, which prohibits turning one's back on the battlefield 'on that day' (yawma'idhin). The question is whether 'that day' includes the night.
The context of battle does not specify time of day.
3
Explain the part-whole relationship
The author argues that 'yawm' consists of moments ('an'). An absolute moment is a part of the day. Since the part (absolute moment) is unrestricted, the whole ('yawm') inherits that unrestricted nature, meaning 'yawm' includes all moments, day and night.
This is based on the principle that the ruling on the part applies to the whole.
4
Derive the ruling
Because 'yawm' in the verse comprises all moments, the prohibition of desertion applies at any time of that day or night.
The ruling is therefore absolute (mutlaq) in time.
5
Note the Hanafi usul principle
The Hanafi approach interprets general terms (amm) according to their full scope unless a specific restriction (takhsis) is proven. Here, no restriction is present, so 'yawm' retains its general sense of a full 24-hour period.
This contrasts with schools that take 'yawm' as daytime only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلق آن مراد ہوگی اور اس کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں کہ یہ آن جز من النہار ہو بلکہ عام مراد ہوگی۔ خواہ جز من النہار ہو یا من الليل كقوله تعالى ومن يولهم - يومئذ دبره تو یوم زحف میں تولی دیر خواہ نہار میں ہو یا لیل میں مطلقا ممنوعا ہے۔ ولان العلاقة موجودة یعنی مطلق آن اور یوم کے معنی حقیقی میں علاقہ موجود ہے اس لئے یوم سے مراد علق آن ہوگی اور علاقہ یہ ہے کہ مطلق آن جز ہے ان یومی کی کیونکہ یوم مخصوص کی آن مقید ہے اور مطلق جز مقید ہوتا ہے اور ان یومی جز من اليوم ہے تو آن مطلق جزمن اليوم ہوگی کیونکہ جزاء الجزء جزء
The absolute 'an' will be intended, and there is no consideration that this 'an' is a part of the day; rather, the general meaning will be intended. Whether it is a part of the day or the night, as in His statement: 'And whoever turns his back to them on that day...' So fleeing on the day of battle is absolutely prohibited whether it is day or night. And because the relationship exists, i.e., between the absolute 'an' and 'yawm' there is a real relationship, so 'yawm' intends the meaning of absolute 'an'. And the relationship is that the absolute 'an' is a part of 'yawm', because the 'an' of a specific day is restricted, and the absolute is a part of the restricted, and 'yawm' is a part of 'day'? So the absolute 'an' will be a part of 'yawm' because the part of the part is a part.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage deals with the usul principle of how general words can be used. The author explains that the word 'yawm' (day) in the Quranic verse 'And whoever turns his back to them on that day' (Quran 8:16) refers to absolute time, not just the daytime. This is because 'yawm' is used for any moment ('an') of the day. The relationship is that the absolute moment is a part of the day, and the ruling of the part applies to the whole. Thus, turning one's back is prohibited whether it happens during the day or night. This is a Hanafi approach to interpreting general terms in context.

Key Terms
آن (an): A moment of time, absolute and unspecified.
یوم (yawm): A day, but in this context used for absolute time including both day and night.

Combining Haqiqah and Majaz in Nadhr and Yamin: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Analyzing the interplay of literal and metaphorical meanings in vows and oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of dalalat al-iltizam to resolve apparent conflicts between literal and metaphorical meanings in performative utterances (insha'at).

Core Idea
A vow (nadhr) is established by wording; an oath (yamin) by intention—they coexist without combining haqiqah and majaz.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced issue in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: how a single utterance like 'I make X haram upon myself' can simultaneously function as a nadhr (vow) and a yamin (oath). The apparent tension between literal and metaphorical meanings is resolved through the concept of dalalat al-iltizam (necessary implication) and the distinct roles of wording vs. intention. We will walk through the commentator's argument, the six scenarios based on intention, and the usul principle that majaz requires a qarinah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Recognize that the wording 'I make X haram' is literally placed for nadhr, yet it can also constitute a yamin (by analogy with the Prophet's honey prohibition and Quran 66:2). The problem is whether this creates a forbidden combination of haqiqah and majaz.
The objection is that if yamin is a necessary consequent (mojib), it should be established without intention, but it requires intention.
2
Distinguish haqiqah from majaz
Haqiqah is the meaning for which the word was coined (nadhr). Majaz is using the word for a related meaning (yamin) with a contextual indicator. In insha'at, haqiqah is established by the sighah alone; majaz by intention.
Hanafi: Nadhr does not require intention; yamin does.
3
Analyze the role of dalalat al-iltizam
The necessary implication (lazim) of the prohibition is that it becomes a yamin. The passage argues that dalalat al-iltizam is not majaz if the literal meaning is also intended. Thus, nadhr is established as haqiqah, and yamin as a necessary concomitant (lazim) without abandoning the literal meaning.
Like 'asad' indicating courage: that's not majaz if the literal 'lion' is also intended.
4
Apply the six scenarios
The passage enumerates situations based on the speaker's intention. Work through each: (1) no intention → only nadhr; (2) nadhr only → nadhr; (3) nadhr + negate yamin → nadhr; (4) both → both; (5) yamin only → yamin? Actually the text says both nadhr and yamin? Check: The passage says for case 5 (intends only yamin) both occur? Wait: The source says 'وان نوى اليمين فقط فندرو يمين' meaning if he intends only yamin, then nadhr and yamin? Actually the translation: 'ان نويا معا (both) فندرو يمين، وان نوى اليمين فقط فندرو يمين'? Let's re-read the Arabic: 'وإن نوى اليمين فقط فندرو يمين' – it says 'فندرو يمين' meaning 'then nadhr and yamin'. But then later it says when he intends yamin and negates nadhr, only yamin. So careful: The passage says: (4) intends both → both; (5) intends only yamin → both? Actually the Arabic: 'وان نوى اليمين فقط فندرو يمين' — this could be interpreted as 'then nadhr and yamin' but the later part clarifies that if he negates nadhr, only yamin. So likely the scenario 'only yamin' without negation yields both. We'll follow the passage as given. For the guided step, we'll say: examine the intention scenarios; note the subtlety when negation is involved.
This shows the precision of Hanafi usul in giving effect to both wording and intention.
5
Recognize the qarinah requirement
For majaz, a qarinah (contextual indicator) is always required. In this issue, the qarinah is the speaker's intention (niyyah) or the external situation. This prevents the literal meaning from being overridden without evidence.
The passage illustrates qarinah with the divorce example: a woman ready to go out restricts the general 'khuruj'.

یعنی جب اس نے مجاز کا ارادہ کیا اور حقیقہ کی نفی کی تو صرف دیا نتہ اس کی تصدیق کی جاسکتی ہے تو یہ شخص فیمابینہ و بین اللہ تعالی صادق شمار کیا جائے گا۔ اور اس مسئلہ میں قضا کو کوئی دخل نہیں ہے کہ قاضی ارادہ حقیقہ سے مانع ہو۔ پھر یہ خواہ عقلاً ہو یا حسا يا عادة یعنی عرفاً يا شرعاً هو وهى اما خارجة الخ یا تو وہ قرینہ ایسا ہوگا جو متکلم اور کلام سے خارج ہوگا ۔ كدلالة المحال جیسے کیمین فور میں دلالت حال قرینہ ہوتا ہے جیسے عورت دروازہ پر نکلنے کے لئے تیار کھڑی ہے خاوند جوش میں آکر کہتا ہے ان خرجت فانت طالق تو یہاں مطلق خروج مراد نہیں ہوگا بلکہ صرف یہی خروج مراد ہوگا جس کے لئے وہ تیار تھی اس کے بعد اگر خروج کرے تو طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی ۔ اد معنی من المتكلم یعنی ایک معنی جو متکلم میں ہے وہ قرینہ مانعہ از حقیقت ہو گا۔ کقولہ تعالیٰ و استفزز
He kept [the vow] and eating food was halal, so with this vow he made those things haram upon himself, then this becomes a yamin (oath), like the Prophet (peace be upon him) made honey haram upon himself, so Allah said: 'Indeed Allah has ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (Surah At-Tahrim 66:2), so He made that prohibition a yamin. Likewise, the purchase of a relative is a form of purchase. But in terms of its consequence (mojib), it is emancipation, i.e., that close relative (dhi rahm mahram) becomes free from purchase. So the summary answer is that this is not combining haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical). Rather, these words, in terms of their form (sighah), are placed for nadhr (vow), and the necessary consequent (mojib/lazim) of this speech is the yamin. So it has a literal meaning and the yamin is a necessary (iltizami) meaning, and the indication of a word to its necessary consequent by way of iltizam is not majaz. Like the word 'asad' when its specific meaning (i.e., the predatory animal) is intended, then courage which is its necessary consequent is indicated by way of iltizam, and this is not majaz, because majaz is when the intended meaning is the necessary consequent of the literal meaning and the literal meaning itself is not intended. That is not the case here. Hence, combining haqiqah and majaz does not become necessary. Now an objection arose in my mind at this point: If this kalam's mojib is that it becomes yamin, then it should become yamin even if he does not intend (yamin) because the mojib of speech is established without intention, like when one purchases a relative, its mojib (emancipation) is established even if he does not intend it, whereas here it is not so; rather, yamin is established with intention and will, and it is not its mojib. If yamin is not the mojib of this speech, then combining haqiqah and majaz becomes necessary. It can be said in answer to this objection that the combining of haqiqah and majaz is not with respect to intention because he intended the yamin and did not intend the nadhr. Thus, no combination occurred in intention. However, the nadhr is established by its wording, which does not require intention and will; hence it is established without intention. The yamin is established by intention. Since the wording is placed for nadhr and it is an insha, its literal meaning is established even without intention, and the yamin (the metaphorical meaning) is established with intention, so no combination in intention occurs. The reality of this answer is that it concedes that yamin is the metaphorical meaning of this speech; in the first answer it was considered the mojib and necessary meaning. But in insha'at (performative utterances), it is possible that both the literal meaning and the metaphorical meaning are established: the literal meaning is established by the mere wording, whether one intends or not, and the metaphorical is established when one intends. So this issue is divided into divisions: (1) He intends neither nadhr nor yamin, (2) He intends only nadhr and not yamin, (3) He intends nadhr and negates yamin – in these three cases only nadhr occurs, acting upon the wording because the wording is placed for nadhr. (4) He intends both nadhr and yamin, (5) He intends only yamin – in both these cases both nadhr and yamin occur: nadhr by the wording, and yamin by intention. (6) If he intends yamin and negates nadhr, then only yamin occurs. This is the objection I brought: In the last case, when he intends yamin and negates nadhr, nadhr should still be established because its establishment is literal by the wording and the meaning of the wording is established even if he later negates it; that is not considered. He answered: We say, since he intended the metaphorical meaning and negated the literal, he is believed in his conscience (diyanatan). So this person is considered truthful between him and Allah. And the court (qada) has no role in this matter, because the judge does not accept his negation and would impose the nadhr by qada since this matter is not subject to qada. This is unlike divorce and manumission, where if one says he intended the metaphorical meaning and negated the literal, he is not believed in court because these are matters between people; the judge's ruling takes precedence. Rule: Majaz necessarily requires a qarinah (contextual indicator) that prevents the literal meaning. This qarinah may be intellectual, sensory, customary (urfan), or legal (sharan). It is either external to the speaker and speech, like the indication of the situation, e.g., a woman ready to go out; the husband says in anger, 'If you go out, you are divorced.' Here the absolute 'khuruj' is not intended, but only this particular 'khuruj' for which she was ready; if she goes out later, divorce does not occur. Or a meaning in the speaker that prevents the literal meaning, like Allah's saying: 'And incite' (Surah Al-Isra 17:64).
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of a specific fiqh issue regarding whether a wording that can be both a vow (nadhr) and an oath (yamin) actually constitutes both, and how the usul principles of haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning) apply. The commentator is expanding upon the matn (Al-Tawdih) to clarify the resolution of an apparent conflict. The core of the discussion is that when a person uses a formulaic expression like 'I make X haram upon myself,' it can simultaneously be a nadhr (because the wording is literally placed for making a vow) and a yamin (because by analogy with the Prophet's prohibition of honey and the Quranic verse in Surah At-Tahrim, such a prohibition becomes an oath). The initial objection is that if the yamin is the necessary consequent (mojib/lazim) of the speech, it should be established without intention, but in practice intention is required. The commentator resolves this by explaining that the nadhr is established by the wording alone (haqiqah), while the yamin is established by intention (as a metaphorical meaning or as a necessary meaning through dalalat al-iltizam). Since they operate on different levels (one from the wording, the other from intention), there is no actual combination of haqiqah and majaz in the intention. The passage then enumerates six scenarios based on the speaker's intention and shows the rulings for each. A final important usul principle is stated: for majaz to be valid, there must be a qarinah (contextual indicator) that prevents the literal meaning. The qarinah can be external (like the situation) or internal (like the speaker's stated intention). This discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of reconciling textual indications and rational analysis in deriving fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship or make something obligatory upon oneself. In this passage, it is a performative utterance (insha) that establishes an obligation by its wording alone.
یمین (yamin): An oath, typically swearing by Allah, but here extended to a prohibition that carries the ruling of an oath (requiring expiation).
حقیقت (haqiqah): Literal meaning; the meaning for which the word was originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; using a word for a meaning other than its literal one with a contextual indicator (qarinah).
قرینہ (qarinah): A contextual indicator that shows the non-literal meaning is intended. It can be intellectual, sensory, customary, or legal; external to the speaker or internal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for yamin to be established in a wording that is literally for nadhr?
A) Only the wording itself, without need for intention.
B) Only intention (niyyah), without need for the wording.
C) Both the wording and intention.
D) Neither; yamin is never established from such a wording.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a performative utterance can simultaneously establish its literal meaning (haqiqah) by the wording and a necessary implication (lazim) by intention without combining haqiqah and majaz.

Silence and Speech Continuity: The Waw in Death-Illness Manumission
Analyzes rulings when a man frees three slaves in death illness with different utterance patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the waw of conjunction indicates simultaneity in continuous speech but sequentiality when separated by silence, and derive the resulting fiqh ruling for a death-illness manumission scenario.

Core Idea
Silence between utterances makes waw sequential; continuous speech makes it simultaneous, altering the share of each freed slave.

This lesson explores how the presence or absence of silence (sukut) between utterances changes the legal interpretation of the conjunctive waw. Using a death-illness manumission case, we see the practical difference: continuous speech frees each slave by one-third, while sequential pauses gradually free them in whole fractions. This illustrates a core usul principle that the structure of speech (nazm al-kalam) affects meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Constraints
The scenario: a man in death illness (marad al-mawt) frees three slaves of equal value. He has no other property and only one heir (a son who confesses the iqrar). Since the freeing is a bequest, it is limited to one-third of the estate.
The equal value simplifies calculation; if values differ, the distribution would adjust.
2
Compare Continuous vs. Paused Speech
If the man says 'I freed this, and this, and this' in one continuous breath (ittisal), the waw indicates simultaneity (qiran). If he pauses after each name (sukut), the waw is interpreted as sequential (ta'aqub).
The key is the presence or absence of a significant silence between utterances.
3
Derive the Legal Consequence
In continuous speech, all three are freed simultaneously, so the total bequest of one-third is divided equally: one-third of each slave is freed. With silence, the first statement stands alone, freeing the entire first slave (one-third of the estate). Then the second statement is conjuncted sequentially, so only half of the second slave is freed (since half of first + half of second = one-third, but first is already fully freed). Similarly, the third yields one-third of the third slave freed.
The sequential result: first slave fully freed, second half freed, third one-third freed.
4
Connect to the Broader Usul Principle
This embodies the principle that speech is understood based on its entirety, and a modifier at the end can change the beginning (al-kalam mu'allaq bi akhirihi). The absence of silence means the waw connects all items, while silence breaks the connection, making each subsequent clause independent.
This principle also applies to other cases, like the marriage of two sisters or the authorization of slave marriages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

کی آپس میں برابر ہے خان اقر متصلا اگر ان تینوں کے متعلق یہ اقرار متصلا کرتا ہے تو ہر غلام کی ثلث ازاد ہوگی دان مسكت فيما بين ذلك اگر درمیان میں سکوت اختیار کرتا ہے یعنی کہتا ہے اعتق ہذا اس کے بعد کچھ دیر خاموش رہتا ہے پھر کہتا ہے و ہذا اور اس کے بعد سکوت کرتا ہے پھر کہتا ہے وہذا تو اسل خاموشی کی صورت میں اول سالم ازاد ہو جائے گا اور ثانی کا نصف اور ثالث کا ثکث آزاد ہوگا لانه لما قال اعتق الى هذا وسكت يعتق کلہ یہ کل آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ ثلث کل مال ہے مفروض یہ ہے کہ ان تمام عبید کی قیمت برابر برابر ہے۔ فاذا قال بعد السکوت پھر جب سکوت کے بعد کہا وہذا وسکت اور اس کے بعد پھر سکوت اختیار کیا فقد عطفه على الاول تو اس کا عطف پہلے پر ہو تو اس کا مقتضا یہ ہے کہ ثانی کی نصف آزاد ہو مع نصف الاول اور اول کی بھی نصف کیونکہ یہ دو نصفیں مل کر مکمل غلام ہو جاتا ہے جو ثلث مال ہے ۔ لكن لما اعتق الدول لیکن چونکہ اول تو آزاد ہو چکا ہے لہذا اس سے رجوع ممکن نہیں ہے لہذا ثانی کی نصف آزاد ہو جائے گی۔ تھ لما قال وهذا پھر کچھ دیر کے بعد ثالث کے متعلق کہا وهذا فموجبه عتق ثلث الثالث مع عشق ثلث كل من الاولين تو مقتضا اس کا یہ ہے کہ ثالث کی ثلث ازاد ہو اور اولین میں سے بھی ہر ایک کی ثلث و ثلث ازاد ہو کیونکہ اسی طرح یہ کل مال کی ثلث بنتی ہے جس میں وصیت نافذ ہو سکتی ہے لیکن اولین سے جو کچھ آزاد ہو چکا ہے اس سے رجوع ممکن نہیں ہے لہذا ثالث کی ثلث ثانی کا نصف اول کل ازاد ہو جائے گا۔ فجعلتموہ تو تم نے اس صورت میں جہاں بلا سکوت متصلاً اقرار کرتا ہے واو کو قران و مقارنتہ کے لئے قرار دیا یہ ایسے ہوا جیسے وہ کہتا اعتقهم الى معالانه لو لم یکن کیونکہ اگر قران کیلئے نہ ہوتی تو ترتیب ثابت ہوتی جیسے مسئلہ سکوت میں تھا قلنا اما لاول یہاں سے جوابات شروع ہیں اول صورت میں یعنی اس صورت میں جب دو لونڈیوں کا نکاح ایک لونڈی ۔ اس کا نکاح علی الحرة ہے لہذا لم تبق الثانية محلا للتوقف تو ثانیہ اس کا محل ہی نہیں ہے کہ اس کا نکاح اس کے عشق پر موقوف رہے فان نکاح الامة على الحرة لا تجوز کیونکہ امہ کا نکاح خرہ پر سرے سے جائزہ ہی نہیں ہے لہذا یہ لونڈی نکاح کے توقف کی محل ہی نہیں رہی تو اس کا نکاح باطل ہو جائیگا۔ اگر چہ حرہ ہو جانے کے بعد دوبارہ نکاح ہو سکتا ہے لیکن پہلا نکاح جو موقوف تھا وہ باطل ہے۔ واما الثاني والثالث ثانی صورت یعنی تزویج اختین اور ثالث یعنی اعتق الى هذا و هذا و هذا فلان الكلام اس لئے کہ یہ ضابطہ ہے کہ کلام اپنے آخر پر موقوف ہو جاتی ہے جب آخر کلام میں کوئی مغیر آجائے جیسے شرط و استثنا و غیره وهنا اور ان دو مسائل یعنی ثانی و ثالث میں کذلک یعنی آخر کلام اول کے لئے مغیر ہے
And if a man says in his death illness: 'I freed this, and this, and this' concerning three slaves — with no heir except this confessing son, and no property besides these slaves, and their values are equal — if he makes the confession continuously, one-third of each slave is freed. If he remains silent between them: he says 'I freed this', then is silent, then says 'and this', then is silent, then says 'and this' — then the first is entirely freed, the second half freed, the third one-third freed. Because when he said 'I freed this' and was silent, the whole slave is freed (as it is one-third of the total property). Then when he says after silence 'and this' and is silent, it is conjuncted to the first, necessitating half of the second be freed along with half of the first, but since the first is already freed, half of the second is freed. Then when he says after a while 'and this', its requirement is one-third of the third be freed along with one-third of each of the first two, but since the first is already freed and half of the second, the result is one-third of the third plus half of the second plus the whole first freed. So you made the waw in the continuous case for simultaneous conjunction, as if he said 'I free them all at once'. Because if not for conjunction, sequence would be established as in the silence case. We say: as for the first case — when two slave women were married to one man without the master's permission, and the master said with conjunctive letter 'I freed this and this' — then when the first was freed, her marriage became valid without needing permission; the second's marriage is against a free woman, so it cannot be suspended; thus it is void. And the second and third cases (marriage of two sisters and freeing of slaves) are similar because the principle is that speech is suspended upon its end when a changer appears at the end, like a condition or exception, etc. And here in those two cases, the end of the speech is a changer for the beginning.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage examines the effect of speech continuity (ittisal) vs silence (sukut) on the interpretation of waw in a death-illness manumission (i'tiq) involving three slaves. The scenario assumes: no heir except one son who confesses the iqrar, no other property, and all slaves equal in value (so the bequest can only take effect from one-third of the estate). If the man says 'I freed this, and this, and this' in one continuous utterance, the waw indicates simultaneity (qiran wa muqarana), so each slave is freed by one-third (since the total freed value equals the valid bequest of one-third). If he pauses after each name, the first freeing is complete (as it stands alone and equals one-third), then the second is conjuncted sequentially, so only half of the second is freed (because the sum of half of first and half of second equals one-third, but since first is already fully freed, only half of second is freed), and similarly for the third. The passage then links this to the earlier case of ratifying marriage of two sisters: the waw in continuous speech indicates simultaneity, voiding both, whereas silence allows sequence. This exemplifies the usul principle that kalam (speech) is understood based on its entirety, and an ending modifier (mughayyir) like 'waw' used in a certain way can change the meaning of the beginning.

Key Terms
سکوت (sukut): Silence or pause between utterances, here causing each statement to be considered separate and sequential.
اتصال (ittisal): Continuity of speech without pause, making the waw indicate simultaneity.
قران (qiran): Conjunction that indicates simultaneous occurrence, as opposed to sequential.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Death illness during which a person's dispositions (like freeing slaves) are limited to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a death-illness, a man says with continuous speech: 'I freed this, and this, and this' (three slaves of equal value, no other property). What is the ruling?
A) All three are fully freed.
B) Each is freed by one-third.
C) The first is fully freed, the second half, the third one-third.
D) Only the first is freed.

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of silence between utterances changes the interpretation of the conjunctive waw from simultaneity to sequentiality, directly affecting the fiqh ruling in death-illness manumission.

The Conjunctive Waw: Sentences vs. Words in Hanafi Usul
Usul rule: effect of waw on modification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'atf of sentences and 'atf of single words, and apply the Hanafi rule that only continuous 'atf of single words allows the last part to modify the earlier ruling.

Core Idea
The waw does not always create simultaneity: conjoined sentences stand alone; conjoined single words can modify each other.

The conjunctive waw (و) is a common Arabic particle, but its legal effect is not uniform. In Hanafi usul, whether the waw creates partnership (مشاركة) between conjoined elements depends on what it conjoins: two complete sentences or two single words within one statement. This lesson unpacks that distinction through concrete examples of emancipation and marriage, showing when a later part can modify an earlier ruling and when it cannot.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conjunction
Determine whether the waw conjoins two complete sentences (جملة) or two single words (مفرد) within one sentence. This is the primary factor.
Example: 'هذه حرة وهذه حرة' = two sentences; 'اجزت نکاح هذا و هذا' = one sentence with two objects.
2
Check for continuity (اتصال)
If there is a silence (سکوت) between the two statements, each is treated as a separate speech act. Continuous speech without silence treats the conjunction as simultaneous.
The passage uses silence to demonstrate sequential effect in emancipation.
3
Determine if the final part modifies the earlier
Only when the conjunction is of single words (مفرد) and continuous does the last part become a modifier (مغیر) for the first. For sentences, each stands alone.
The key is whether the speech is 'dependent on its end' (موقوف على الآخر).
4
Apply to the marriage cases
In the slave girl case (امتین), the master says 'This is free and this is free' – two sentences, so the first freeing is complete before the second. In the sisters case, 'I permit the marriage of this and this' – one permission with two objects, so the second marriage invalidates the first.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in the first, first marriage valid, second invalid; in the second, both invalid.
5
Account for the opposing view
Some (like al-Husayri) argued no difference between the cases, but the Hanafi response points to the grammatical structure as the reason for the distinction.
This shows how usul principles derive from linguistic analysis.

اور نکاح دونوں کا صحیح ہو جاتا ۔ وقد تدخل بين الجملتين اور واو عاطفہ کبھی بین الجملتين داخل ہوتی ہے فلا توجب المشاركة اس وقت میں وہ موجب مشاکر نہیں ہوگی بلکہ صرف دونوں جملے کسے ہوں مضمون میں جمع کا فائدہ دے گی ۔ ہر ایک جملہ اپنے اپنے مضمون کا الگ طور پر مفید ہوگا - ففى قوله هذه طالق ثلثا وهذه طالق تو اس صورت میں ثانیہ مطلقہ بطلاق واحدہ ہوگی جب کہ اولی مطلقہ بطلاق ثلثہ ہوگی ثانیہ شریک بالا ولی فی الطلقات الثلث
He chooses silence, meaning he says 'I free this,' then remains silent for a while, then says 'and this,' and after that he remains silent, then says 'and that' — in this case of silence, the first becomes entirely free, the second half, and the third one-third. Because when he said 'I free this' and was silent, he frees all of it, and this is one-third of his total wealth, assuming the values of all these slaves are equal. When he said after the silence 'and this' and was silent, then after silence he said 'and that,' he conjoined it to the first. So its requirement is that half of the second is freed along with half of the first, and also half of the first, because these two halves together make a complete slave, which is one-third of wealth. But since the first has already been freed, it is not possible to revert, so half of the second becomes free. When he said 'and this' after some time regarding the third, the consequence is that one-third of the third is freed along with one-third of each of the first two. This would form one-third of the total wealth in which bequest is valid. But since part of the first two is already freed, reversion is not possible, so one-third of the third and half of the second with the entire first become free. So you made the waw to indicate conjunction and simultaneity in the case where he makes the declaration continuously without silence, as if he said 'I free them all at once.' Because if it were not for conjunction, sequentiality would be established as in the case of silence. We say: As for the first case — the case where two slave girls are married to one person without the master's permission, and the master said with the particle of conjunction 'I freed this and this' — in this case, when the first (Hind) was freed, her marriage was concluded and the master's permission was no longer needed. As for the second slave girl, her marriage is to a free woman, so the second does not remain a locus for suspension, because marriage of a slave woman to a free woman is not permissible. So this slave girl is no longer a locus for suspension of marriage, so her marriage becomes invalid. Even if she becomes free later, the marriage can be renewed, but the first marriage that was suspended is invalid. As for the second and third — the second case is the marriage of two sisters, and the third is the saying 'I free this, and this, and this' — because the rule is that speech is dependent on its end when something modifying (mugheer) appears at the end, like a condition or exception etc. Here in these two issues (second and third) it is likewise: the final part is a modifier for the first. As for the two sisters issue, the permission of the second marriage invalidates the first marriage, so the final changed the ruling of the first. Therefore, with the conjunction, both marriages become invalid. As for the informing by emancipation — the one who informs says 'I freed my father this,' and this statement necessitates the emancipation of all of this slave. Then his statement 'and this' necessitates that the emancipation is divided between these two slaves and part of the first is freed, so it becomes a modifier of the first speech. And the modifier is considered only when it occurs continuously, unlike the case of the two slave girls. In the case of the two slave girls, the final speech is not a modifier for the first because in 'I freed this and this' the freeing of the second cannot be a modifier for the freeing of the first. So here the final speech is not a modifier for the first. In the two sisters issue, the final speech is a modifier, so it is dependent on the end. And it is mentioned in al-Majami' al-Husayri: In the issue of the two slave girls and the issue of the two sisters, there is no difference. Rather, difference arose only due to difference in the structure of the issue. That is: in the issue of the two slave girls, the master says 'This is free and this is free' — conjoining a sentence to a sentence. So with the first sentence the slave girl is freed and her marriage becomes valid. When he finishes the second sentence, the marriage of the slave girl to a free woman is not allowed, so it becomes invalid. In the issue of the two sisters, he says 'I permit the marriage of this and this' — conjoining a single word to a single word. He validates both marriages with a single permission. So in the issue of the two slave girls, each slave girl is separately freed; therefore the beginning of the speech is not dependent on the end, so it is not a modifier. In the issue of the two sisters, there is no separate permission for each, so it is a single permission; therefore the first speech is dependent on the end, so both marriages become invalid. Even if he had given separate permission in the two sisters issue, e.g., saying 'I permit the marriage of this and I permit the marriage of this,' the first marriage would be valid. And if he had not freed separately in the two slave girls issue but said 'I freed this this,' both would be freed together and both marriages would be valid. And the conjunction 'waw' sometimes enters between two sentences and does not necessitate partnership; it only gives the benefit of combining the two sentences in meaning. Each sentence independently conveys its own meaning. For example, in the saying 'This is thrice divorced and this is divorced' — in this case, the second woman is divorced with a single divorce while the first is divorced with three divorces; the second does not share in the three divorces.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage, from a Hanafi usul sharh, explains the differential effect of the conjunctive waw when conjoining sentences versus conjoining single words, and when the conjunction is continuous versus interrupted by silence. The author first illustrates with a case of sequential emancipation with silence: when the master says 'I free this' then pauses, the first slave is fully freed; then after a pause 'and this' frees half the second; after another pause 'and this' frees one-third of the third plus makes adjustments to keep total within one-third of wealth. The principle is that silence creates separate speech acts, so each statement is independent. Then the passage contrasts this with continuous conjunction without silence, where the waw indicates simultaneity, and the last part can modify the earlier. This leads to a detailed discussion of two marriage cases: (1) a master frees two slave girls married to one man, and (2) a master permits the marriage of two sisters. In the first, because each freeing is a separate sentence ('This is free, and this is free'), the first freeing is complete before the second, so the first marriage is valid; the second marriage (to a free woman) is void ab initio because it's impermissible, so the second freeing doesn't affect the first. In the second case, the permission is a single sentence conjoining two direct objects ('I permit the marriage of this and this'), so it's one act; the last part can modify the first, making the first marriage dependent on the second. Since marrying two sisters simultaneously is invalid, both marriages are invalid. The passage cites al-Majami' al-Husayri to confirm that the difference stems from the grammatical structure: conjoining sentences vs conjoining individual words. Finally, it notes that if waw conjoins two separate sentences, it doesn't create partnership in ruling; each sentence stands alone (e.g., 'This is thrice divorced and this is divorced'). All examples are thoroughly Hanafi, adhering to the madhhab's stance that the effect of 'atf depends on continuity and the nature of the conjoined units.

Key Terms
عطف (atf): Conjoining: joining two items (sentences or words) with a conjunction (waw).
واو عاطفہ (waw atifah): The conjunctive waw, used to connect elements, but its effect depends on what it conjoins.
مغیر (mugheer): A modifier: a later part of speech that changes the ruling of an earlier part.
جملہ (jumla): A complete sentence; when conjoined, each maintains independent legal effect.
مفرد (mufrad): A single word; when conjoined, the conjoined items share the same ruling as if part of one act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this passage, in which case does the final part of a conjunctive statement modify the earlier ruling?
A) When the waw conjoins two complete sentences (جملہ) continuously.
B) When the waw conjoins two single words (مفرد) continuously.
C) When there is a pause (سکوت) between the two statements.
D) When the waw conjoins two sentences with a pause.

Key Takeaway
The effect of the conjunctive waw depends on whether it conjoins sentences (independent) or single words (modification possible), and on continuity.

Does Conjunction of Sentences Imply Shared Ruling? The Case of Zakat on Minors
Debates whether conjunction of sentences implies shared ruling, applied to zakat on minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'aṭf al-jumal and argue why the verse 2:43 does not necessitate zakat on minors.

Core Idea
Conjunction of sentences does not automatically transfer ruling; the second clause must be dependent on the first.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul dispute: does connecting two sentences with 'waw' mean they share the same legal ruling? This issue has practical consequences for whether zakat is wajib on minors. We will explore the opposing view based on Quranic verse 2:43, and the Hanafi response that separates the proof of obligation from the mere conjunction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What exactly is being disputed? Focus on whether conjunction of sentences (عطف الجمل) implies sharing in the same ruling (حكم).
The dispute is about the implication of the waw of conjunction, not about the specific fiqh ruling itself.
2
State the opposing view and its proof
Opponents argue that conjunction in wording necessitates correspondence in ruling. They cite Quran 2:43 ('Establish prayer and give zakat') and reason that since prayer is not obligatory on minors, zakat also is not. Their underlying proof: the addressee of both commands is the same.
They deduce the ruling on minors indirectly, via the conjunction.
3
State the Hanafi view and its alternative proof
Hanafis agree that zakat is not wajib on minors, but they reject the reasoning from conjunction. Instead, they argue that zakat is 'ibada mahda (pure worship) without an element of hardship, and minors lack capacity for such obligations. The conjunction does not necessitate shared ruling; rather, the ruling is derived independently.
The Hanafi proof is based on the nature of the act ('ibada mahda), not the grammar.
4
Analyze the linguistic example
The passage illustrates with: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free.' Here, the second clause ('my slave is free') shares the condition because it is dependent on the first; it is treated like a singular word in need of the condition. If the predicate were repeated, it would indicate independence.
This example shows when sharing occurs (need) and when it does not (independence).
5
Conclude with thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis hold that zakat is not obligatory on a minor with wealth; the opposing school holds that it is obligatory (and can be paid by the guardian).
Remember: the disagreement arises from a different usul principle about conjunction.
